		<date>2009-03-20</date>
		<username>Bill Slocum (bill.slocum@gmail.com)</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>Animal Farm</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Buying cigarettes in a pub, David Sumner sees his wife Amy accosted by a former boyfriend. Does he run outside and rip the guy's ears off? Or does he take her cue and play it off as if nothing happened?Answer two is the wrong one, apparently, if you are director Sam Peckinpah and the subject is marriage. "Straw Dogs" is the director's most problematic problem film, not because it's bad but because it's good, just very confusing, and in my view anyway, confused.Dustin Hoffman and Susan George star as the couple, trying to establish themselves in her old Cornish hometown so he can pursue his career as a mathematician in relative peace. The film is replete with references to the violence of the times, Vietnam and street riots."See anybody get knifed?" he is asked by a rat-catching creep named Cawsey (Jim Norton)."Just between commercials," David replies.Of course, violence is never too far from the surface in a Peckinpah movie, especially here with a beautiful woman who likes pushing buttons and is a little put out by her weeny spouse. Using the primitivist vernacular Peckinpah worked from here, David's hunter-gatherer skills are found to be too much gatherer, not enough hunter. Susan craves a firmness he lacks, especially when Cawsey and the other creeps rebuilding a garage on the Sumners' farm are the likely suspects for a particularly nasty B&amp;E. You see Amy's point when she calls David a "coward", but it's hard to like her, even with George giving the film's best performance."You're an animal," David jokes several times to his wife, but to Amy it's no joke but an accurate observation. If he wants to keep her, the film seems to say, he better get animal, too.Is Peckinpah making a "fascist film", as New Yorker critic Pauline Kael once famously observed? I think he's making a confused one. He wants to indict the culture of violence the Sumners spring from, but makes David a fence-sitter rather than an activist when it comes to the big issues of the day. Amy eventually is victimized by the creeps, but there's a degree of culpability on her part that's less ambiguous than it is frustratingly ill-resolved.There's bravado Sam moments all the same. The final fight at the farm, where both David and his attackers revert to their animal origins in a fight to the death, is riveting, and even more so is a preliminary sequence held at a local church where nothing much happens but Peckinpah the control artist keeps you glued to every snigger and stare.Peckinpah claimed later that David is the film's villain, which the commentator on the Criterion DVD I watched took great pains to affirm. I don't see it. David's patronizing, and a bit smug, but not taking his side in the last thirty minutes of the film is more than a bit perverse.Ambiguity is the rule here, though; it's hard being definitive about anything where "Straw Dogs" is concerned. The final scene pits David against the creeps, both using deadly violence for a cause that on both ends we know to be rather flawed. Not that I didn't root for David all the same; Hoffman may not be as interesting as George in this film but he's a compelling underdog.The film works terrifically at building tension, far more so than at relieving it. It's not Peckinpah's best, or even one of his best, but it's a good, atypical film from the heart of his most productive period, utilizing a new cinematographer, new composer, new locale (not North America, for the first and next-to-last time), and new editors, who obviously helped him create some killer sequences. Peckinpah films usually reused familiar actors from his other films, but "Straw Dogs" only has one who appeared in a film made before or after: David Warner, who plays Henry Niles the village idiot.No question Peckinpah had his hang-ups with women, and in "Straw Dogs" he lets them all hang out. It's a flawed film for that, yet Peckinpah creates a film that grabs you and doesn't let go. It's not a film that hangs together all that well, but it stays with you long after the credits roll, a sign of a true artist at work, even if he doesn't know quite where he's going.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>jamesraeburn2003</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>An important part of movie history</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>In a Cornish village a mild mannered and seemingly eccentric mathematician called David Sumner (Hoffman) is subjected to harassment and bullying by the narrow minded locals who raped his wife Amy (George). Pushed to breaking point he takes a violent revenge to defend his home.STRAW DOGS is based on a novel called THE SIEGE AT TRENCHER'S FARM by Gordon Williams. The director Sam Peckinpah never explained to the audience what the new title meant but the press informed everyone that it was taken from a Chinese proverb. The picture was extremely controversial at the time because of it's violence and the notorious rape scene which was depicted as if Amy was asking for it. As a result the film was banned for nearly twenty years when it was denied a video release under the 1984 Video Recordings Act. It has now received a DVD release in it's uncut version.STRAW DOGS is a violent film and the rape scene is unpleasant, but far worse violence has been unleashed on to our cinema screens in recent years, but having said that I still wouldn't want my eight year old son watching it. The level of acceptable violence and sexuality depicted on screen was far less lax back in 1971 (that's thirty-three years ago!). Viewing the film today one can understand the controversy that the picture caused when one thinks back to the way the world was all those years ago. As a film STRAW DOGS is an important moment of movie history in these respects and as a film it has it's delights. Dustin Hoffman is excellent as the mild mathematician pushed to the limit and Susan George is good as his wife. Sam Peckinpah assembled a first rate British crew to make this film. The supporting cast features many versatile British actors including Peter Vaughan, David Warner, Colin Welland and Peter Arne and the cinematography of John (Witchfinder General) Coquillon is absolutely stunning. He makes superb use of the west of England locations.</fullReview>

		<username>virek213</username>
		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Peckinpah crosses into horror</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>After having strongly altered the western film genre with his first five movies, the notorious Sam Peckinpah struck out on new territory. STRAW DOGS was his first film in a contemporary setting, the first to be shot somewhere other than North America, and, arguably, his first and only foray into the horror genre (though BRING ME THE HEAD OF ALFREDO GARCIA has elements of horror in it too).The film's horror is primarily sociological and psychological. Dustin Hoffman portrays an American mathematician on sabbatical in the Cornwall region of England with his British-born wife (Susan George). Ostensibly, he is here to continue his studies in peace; in reality, however, he has come to escape the campus unrest in America caused by the Vietnam war. But he won't find a whole lot of peace even in rural England, as the five workmen he has hired to work on his house have now started a campaign of painful humiliation.The first incident involves George's pet cat getting strangled (very horrible). The second involves the men, led by Del Henney, leading Hoffman out to the moor on a duck hunt--and then having Henney and a second man (Ken Hutchison) brutally rape George; this scene spawned the film's biggest firestorm, because many critics thought George enjoyed the rape. This, however, is not something I believe was the case. In any event, none of these horrific events causes Hoffman to do anything (and George doesn't even tell him about the rape).But when Hoffman takes in a mentally incapacitated man (David Warner) who he accidentally ran into in the fog, things change. For Warner is being sought by the local town drunk (Peter Vaughan) for molesting his daughter, however unwittingly. When Vaughan and the workmen show up at Hoffman's isolated farmhouse demanding that he hand Warner over to them, Hoffman refuses. The result is a nightmarish siege, punctuated by screams, shotgun blasts, and spurting blood, as Hoffman decimates all of his enemies. The coda, in which he and Warner drive off in the fog, is chilling and ambiguous.STRAW DOGS is not an easy movie to like, because Peckinpah's ideas about Man's violent nature are far too realistic and uncomfortable, even for today's gruesome standards. Still, this is a shocking masterpiece, especially due to the fine and ominous cinematography of John Coquillon, who lensed a number of low-budget British horror movies of the time (including THE WITCHFINDER GENERAL), and an excellent, Stravinsky-influenced music score by Jerry Fielding. Just on those two counts alone, STRAW DOGS qualifies as a horror film with a stunning subtext to match.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>An unfortunate collection of caricatures</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>A collection of caricatures go through the motions of a terrible script, phoning in even the most dramatic moments, spoiling an exciting premise. I'm not even referring to the Cornwall yokels here, who are at least amusing; it's the two main characters who end up being the greatest let-downs, along with the simplistic morality play of a story itself. To its credit, the film is gorgeous, full of beautiful countryside and rustic town, but that can hardly carry two hours of melodrama.Dustin Hoffman plays a perfect effete professor stereotype, a self-centered coward smugly certain of his superiority, and due to that, the near-instant switch into unbeatable gladiator makes little sense. An explosion of rage and dishing out a few good hits before going down, perhaps. This is no Falling Down, where someone on the verge of cracking for a long time finally does; this is someone who becomes savage against his nature, and somehow conjures the skill to kill every enemy. A male superhero fantasy.Susan George is competent as a petulant brat, always needing attention, pushy yet unwilling to take personal action; the nature of their relationship or why they left is never explained, but it's hinted to be teacher/student. Unfortunately, her character never really goes anywhere, and her acting never gives any nuance to even the charged, violent scene preceding the rape. The only time she seems convincing is in the bedroom scene following David's return.The bizarre direction of the rape, that quiet tenderness suggesting that the first wasn't really rape after all, that deep down an attention-seeking girl is really looking for masculine violence from her paramour that she isn't getting from her husband, really confuses me... almost a rape fantasy. And then the whole purpose of the rape is just to create some extra justification for killing the louts, along with audience titillation. In 1971, it may have been shocking to put an on-screen rape in a mainstream movie, kicking off Last House on the Left and a whole series of rape-revenge movies, but now it just looks exploitative and badly done.Rounding out the cast, there's a few louts, an affable retired army major, a teen temptress, her drunken old father, and a retarded pedophile. Their names are hardly important, because all they do is fill a role.Beyond that, no action in the film looks real. The hits don't look real. The deaths don't look real. The rape doesn't look real.Overall, there's nothing in here but a generic western, uprooted and moved to Scotland, and any nuance the story could have had is missing.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Was the Straw Dogs DVD banned in the UK?</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>A number of contributors suggest that Straw dogs was banned in the UK. This is a little misleading - I certainly saw it in a cinema soon after it was released (Clockwork Orange was also not refused a certificate - it was withdrawn by Kubrick). Page 61 of the 2002 annual report of the British Board of Film Classification (http://www.bbfc.co.uk/downloads/ files/BBFC_AnnualReport_2002.pdf) points out that the film was passed uncut in 1971 for cinema showings in the UK. A DVD version was refused a certificate in 1999, but the version submitted was one in which cuts had been made for the US market. The cuts were deemed to distort the impact of the rape scene. When an uncut version was submitted with all the rape scene it was passed in full in 2002.</fullReview>

		<username>Tom May (joycean_chap@hotmail.com)</username>
		<score>0.5</score>
		<shortDescription>A momentarily beguiling, more often numbly disturbing film.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Spoiler Alert First aired on British television but weeks ago, "Straw Dogs" is a distinctly odd and uneasy film. Peckinpah is a considerable 'name director' and I gather is one of the most impressive and complex explorers of the Western genre. "Straw Dogs" certainly reflects his interest in depicting violence. Crucially however, the setting is alien to Peckinpah; distant Britain in a presumably fictionalised Cornish locale.I'm not too surprised that one of the said reasons for this film's ban was the pressure of the Cornish tourist board; a well-trodden path of shrill giggles, sinister glances and bullish swaggering is traversed by the locals. Of course, they resemble in the later stages, a mob, breaking into Hoffman's house. What so disturbs in this film is the sense that we seem meant to applaud David's improvised, horrific violence. While we can sympathise with this mild-mannered, slightly beleaguered character earlier in the film, the precise mania of the closing section seems entirely alien. Defending his household (and an unwitting murderer ensconced inside) is raised to an abstract ideal. David's very manhood is confirmed, Peckinpah seems to assert. But how hollow it all is, with Hoffman's unpatterned performance in the later stages. There seems to be little communication between director and actor as to how to play the part.This leads into another intensely problematic area of the film; the portrayal of the sole substantial female role; Amy, as played by Susan George. That David obtains a victory at the close that asserts violent masculine dominance of the household would matter much more if we could really care about and understand Amy. But indeed, we cannot; her reactions to the respective rapes are decidedly jarring and unexplained. The qualitative 'difference' between the two transgressions bemuses and possibly appals. Peckinpah's portrayal of George's character seems lacking in empathy; she is seen as bringing it all on herself, with her minxish, provocative behaviour. Yet, there are minor attempts early on to make her seem an intelligent, sympathetic girl. At times you feel for her (the church gathering where she seems to be experiencing trauma), but you cannot understand things such as her relation to the villagers or her feelings about the rapes. Susan George tries very hard, but is left adrift by Peckinpah's sheer disinterest in her character; there are only scant clues as to her motivation and feelings, most of the time. What a missed opportunity it is that she isn't set against her husband as a model of anti-violence. But Peckinpah does not seem interesting in making anything like a coherent comment on the violent events.Viewed dispassionately, the film is pretty well made; there is quite an effective visual sense of setting in the house and the surrounding countryside. The image of Peter Vaughan in his scotch-soaked beard and bulk impresses itself on the mind, even if the character is a cipher. The early playfulness of Susan George pulling faces and playing chess charms, and we can accept her impudent pouting as part of her character. It is a shame that from a certain point onwards (her reaction to the first rape) we are completely distanced from understanding her; Amy becomes an all-o'er-the-place pawn for various bits of action. There are certainly some beautiful shots of Hoffman watching events in a street, quite early in the film, taking in shop fronts and tottering villagers. Whilst David Warner and T.P. McKenna are entirely ill-served, an amusing and characterful turn slips under the net in the form of Colin Welland's hapless, slightly absurd showman vicar. It must be admitted, also, that Peckinpah creates a powerful effect with the build-up to the rape, with the image fracturing starkly as the villager strikes Amy. This hits the harder and moves far more than the more 'set-piece' finale with Hoffman's character 'defending his home'/committing brutish murders. It is inexplicable that the initial rape is very possibly enjoyed by Amy, after the sinister, harrowing lead-up to it, emphasised by the fractured shot.Obviously, "Straw Dogs" is an inauthentic film, with little sense of naturalism in the setting, and must be taken as such. But, it is surely a shame that Peckinpah couldn't draw a bit more from the Cornish locale, and particularly his characters. There seems to be little real interest for him than an exploration of the means and carnage of the violence. So, a decidedly curious and questionable film, but it certainly should not be obscured from public view. Although, hopefully, few will draw any negative conclusions about life in distant outposts of the British Isles, or approve of the neolithic view of women and tacit support for vigilante violence.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>Director Sam Peckinpah never really was known for the subtlety or charitably in his films, but he has really surpassed himself with the cruelty "Straw Dogs". Starting random fights, carrying around shotguns, assaulting other men's wives and taking the law in your own hands?all these awry things are portrayed like they're something you have to do in order to become a REAL man. The absolute height of this wrongful illustration unquestionable is the despicable rape-sequence halfway in the film. Many 70's movies handled about rape &amp; revenge ("Last House on the Left", "Deliverance", "I Spit on your Grave", "A Clockwork Orange"?), but "Straw Dogs" is the only one of the bunch that approaches the themes as completely impeccable subjects. David and Amy Sumner are initially introduced as introvert and more educated people, but apparently this isn't good enough for Sam Peckinpah! Real males drink, fight, work with their hands and dominate females. Killing everyone who perpetrates his property at the end of the movie looks almost like a reward for David; a sign of manhood. Disgusting! Does all this mean that "Straw Dogs" is a bad movie and/or unworthy of his classic reputation? Well, the answer is no and that's the tricky part! No matter how much I'd like to warn people NOT to watch it, I can't deny that it's a very well made film and compelling film. The build up towards the inevitably violent climax is truly fascinating. Our young couple initially encounters antipathetic behavior of the Cornish community, then hostility and only then pure inhumanity. Our controversial director masterfully illustrates these switches in tone by simple sound and editing-tricks. The scene where Amy is raped, for example, gets constantly interfered by images of David walking home from hunting, totally unaware that he was lured out of his house. Also undeniable is the influence "Straw Dogs" had on future controversial cinema. This film is completely aware of its controversy but doesn't give a damn whether or not people have moral issues it. This in-your-face attitude somewhat became a standard rule in later cult films.</fullReview>

		<date>2009-01-12</date>
		<username>Jacques98</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>One of the most realistic portrayals of emotion on film.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>In the early 2000s, there was a breakout of movies labeled as "torture porn", which all had three main factors. First, they were intended to make the audience feel the same utter desperation as the tortured characters on screen. Second, they almost always had a deep social portrayal. And, third, they were always rejected by hypocritical critics who failed to see intelligence in desperation, then went off and praised hollow "fine cinema" pieces for "saying something about the human condition". These types of films have always interested me because they mix unrelenting pace with a non-genetic message about how humans tend to think. And I find it interesting that Straw Dogs (1971), while not really a "torture" film, has every single aspect I just listed. While it's gained a lot more credit today, in its time, it was just as hated by the critics as "torture porn" is now. It's funny how easily society can completely be in denial when a movie like Straw Dogs says something about the human condition no one will dare say, while society can then go and praise some completely hollow, cliché-spewing film like (excuse the modern example) No Country for Old Men. I find that hypocrisy almost comical. And I think, maybe, that was one of Straw Dog's points.So what makes Straw Dogs so intelligent? Well, first, it is simply one of the few accurate portrayals of REAL human emotion in cinema. Realism is a word that is thrown around constantly by writers/directors, but as Hollywood gets closer to what it thinks is realism, it just takes five steps back from being truly anything like how real humans think. Very few movies have ever achieved truly expressing how people interact without turning the characters into some podium to preach some idea the writer/director has, or just turning the characters into pieces of cardboard that move the plot along. Contrary to popular belief, the greatest acting in the world can't fix unrealistic characters. That works just about as much as a pretty coat of paint fixes a house that's ready to collapse in on itself. The emotion is Straw Dog's shining point. The two main characters' emotions are portrayed differently in every situation. One scene will end with a loving moment, then the next will open with a bitter one, then the next will open with completely indifference. Things that should have an emotional impact on the characters doesn't have any whatsoever. Actions that should cause them negative emotion cause them pleasure. Just when you find a character totally likable, they'll do something to ruin that feeling?a lot like the betrayal of a friend. Put simply: I've seen countless movies in my life, but never once have I seen a movie with this much of a realistic emotional core.Likewise, without spoiling anything, Straw Dogs goes where no movie in its time dared to go with its subject matter. While I wouldn't necessary call every idea presented here original, some of them are, and not a single one of them is a cliché. It's very relatable to A Clockwork Orange?which came out the same year?in that way. I find it sad, however, that A Clockwork Orange is now considered some sort of classic, while Straw Dogs is still lesser known to the general public than a lot of foreign indie films. Straw Dogs nearly singlehandedly formed the groundwork for the thriller genre, and its influence can be seen in everything from other 70s movies to whatever cliché thriller is playing at the local theater as I type this. The ending is pure intensity, and very few movies can pull that off. The kills in the end of the film are a lot more graphic than anything I expected from a 70s film, and some are just brutal. Straw Dogs deserves more recognition.So, if my review is entirely positive, why do I not give this a higher score? As much as I can relate to this movie, and as much as I appreciate it, I think giving this a perfect score is an insult to what the director was going for. Sam Peckinpah didn't want this film to be entertaining; he wanted it to truly disturb the viewer. Though that may be a little hard to do now in 2009, due to the countless rip-offs and rehashes of the subject matter, this is nowhere near easy viewing. Perfection is an extension of contentment, and I personally was not content with this movie. I wasn't supposed to be. It's a point-blank contradiction to the Hollywood formula that states you have to make the viewer go in their pants out of awe/contentment or you haven't done your job. That's why I can't give Straw Dogs a perfect score, but it didn't want one. It's too honest for that. It's too intelligent for that.7/10</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Straw Dogs</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>In 1971 Sam Peckinpah made the bitter, internally aggressive film Straw Dogs. Starring Dustin Hoffman and Susan George, Peckinpah injected such harsh realism in Straw Dogs which tells the story of a young couple who moved to England to avoid the violence becoming common in America. Isolation and class struggles are the driving issues in Straw Dogs and Peckinpah explores such issues thoroughly and with ease.David Sumner (Dustin Hoffman) and his wife Amy (Susan George) are settling into their new home in Amy's native England. Fearing that America had grown too violent, pacifist David seeks to retreat to a more peaceful community. Quickly noticing many divisions in the area, the drunks/sober people, upper/lower class, David realizes idyllic England may not be as peaceful as he thought it would be. Being an astrophysicist, David enjoys a certain wealth and status that those working on his house do not. The more he is exposed to the stark class differences, the more David realizes how different England is than he thought. When a series of strange events happen in and around David and Amy's house, they both begin to believe the impoverished workers repairing their home are to blame. When David attempts to learn more about the workers and goes duck hunting with them, Amy, left home alone, is raped by two of the men. What next ensues is a violent brawl between David attempting to protect his wife and his home against the attack of the hostile men on the outside. Peckinpah spares no expense exposing the violence that surrounds everyday life, and that so often presents itself in the class conflicts that absorb life. Such elements are crucial to explore, and Peckinpah leaves no stone unturned in his raw exploration of isolation and class struggles. As effective as this exploration proves, it falls short with the casting of Dustin Hoffman. I just don't buy him as a leading actor in any capacity ever. He comes off so one-dimensional, especially in this film. We need a somewhat quiet and unassuming lead actor, but Hoffman is not the actor. The movie was paced very slowly and becomes difficult to engage with. With no likable characters anywhere within the film, the slow pacing makes it even more difficult to engage in. A better film with the same bitter personal deterioration is Joel Schumacher's 1993 film Falling Down, starring Michael Douglas. Likewise, there are no likable characters to attach to in that film either, but it is paced well enough to become engaged in watching the story unfold. I recognize the importance of Peckinpah's Straw Dogs, but it struggles with poor pacing and Dustin Hoffman.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Explaining Today's Shooting Sprees</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I never saw a film so simple and yet so complex at the same time. This is how sophisticated Sam Peckinpah was as director of Straw Dogs. Violence can be a result of mutual contempt without you being conscious of helping to develop one. His analysis of social problems is second to none. You can even restructure all governments' crime-preventing policy based upon his films, particularly on this one. Someone made the right call not to try any sequels of this film. 		<shortDescription>Peckinpah at his best</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>After courting controversy with the bloody western The Wild Bunch in 1969, filmmaker Sam Peckinpah would push the envelope of on screen violence even further with his 1971 follow up film Straw Dogs. The story of a timid mathematician who turns into a blood thirsty killer after a group of hoodlums rape his wife and attack his home, the film remains a visceral and disturbing watch some 45 years after its original release.Straw Dogs follows mathematician David Sumner (Dustin Hoffman) and his attractive young wife Amy (Susan George) as they settle in Amy's quiet childhood village in Cornwall so that David can devote time to his work. Markedly different within the town's milieu, the Sumner's presence causes dissension among the townspeople with a gang of local hoodlums beginning to bully Sumner and lust after his wife.The situation escalates when one of the lead hoodlums rapes Amy, setting the stage for the film's second half where David finally sets aside his "nice guy" facade in order to savagely reassert his masculine pride.Violent and unrelenting, Straw Dogs is a meditation on man's base savagery from one of cinema's most terminally politically incorrect auteurs. Whether one finds themselves loving or hating Peckinpah's polarizing vision, however, Straw Dogs is certainly not a film to leave you indifferent, a testament to its enduring power so many years after its initial release.</fullReview>

Like the protagonist, the viewer too finds himself in a very hard and rustic environment. Straw dogs pulls of the disturbing act finely, the editing and juxtaposition of violent montages between the regular chorus of things agitates the viewer as much it does so to the characters. The violence is stylistic, specially as we are drawn to the climax the amalgamation of stylistic violence together with the soundtrack creates an ambiance so intriguing that you find your eyes glued to the screen. The entire climax is dark, disturbing and perfectly executed. As for the much debated sexually explicit scene, it is alarming and at the same time confusing. The entire plethora of emotions radiated by Susan George through out the scene never quite lets the viewer settle in on a conclusion of an emotion of his own. Rage, Guilt, Agony and a whole lot of emotion rush in and the editing adds to the discomfort all the more. As for performances, it was a fair show. The leads Susan and Dustin do a fine job. Dustin in fact fits right into it, a marvelous all round acting display. The transition displayed towards the climate required a subtle precision that only an actor of Dustin s caliber could have pulled off and this is precisely what he does. It was a very flat character to start with, not much time was invested and it never drew as much sympathy or connection as the story might have required. Above and beyond that it was a thrilling experience. I'd give it a 8/10 and would strictly recommend it if you wish to see how an audacious director brought about the change in cinema and pushed the boundaries.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Slow Burn to Inferno</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This is one of those Movies that has sadly diminished in its power over time. It was inevitable. But at the time it had a charge that was undeniable. When audiences came out of the Theatre in 1971 they were not the same as when they went in. Few knew the impact that was about to be unleashed on the screen.At the time the "new" violence was just starting to emerge but was only in a smattering of Films and it still could surprise and shock and was at its most effective when coming out of the blue or in an unsuspecting format. Westerns, Horror, War, and Police Movies were starting to include the ultra-violence almost routinely. So for the most part it was expected.But here we have a Drama, a sort of Soap-Opera Story of Newlyweds in the Countryside on a Sabbatical/Vacation. It starred the effeminate Dustin Hoffman and it seemed like a Character study of Marriage and young love relationships. Or so the audience thought. But it was more than that, much more. It ended up being a struggle for Manhood and a penalty for flirtatious, immature behavior erupting, after some very slow burns into one of the most unexpected displays of Cinematic carnage yet witnessed.You can't go back, and things are never the same. But this one was an explosive barrier breaker and changed Movie styles and graphic violence and Screen expectations forever. A seminal work that even today can be quite challenging and impressive. It is at times quite uncomfortably tense and disturbing, but it is a masterwork of an unrestrained uberism.NOTE: Forget the remake, it is a virtually worthless rehash of almost scene for scene without the grit and grind of the original.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>No Good Guys Left</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>It's ironic to me that Sam Peckinpah was brought to task during his heyday for being an irresponsible peddler of movie violence. Because compared to just about any movie being released today with a PG-13 rating or higher, Peckinpah's films seem almost cautionary in their depiction of violence and the consequences it has, both mentally and physically, on both the the dealers and recipients of it.In "Straw Dogs," his harrowing 1971 film starring Dustin Hoffman and Susan George, a bookish professor moves with his young wife to England for the peace and quiet they can't find in the volatile U.S. What they find is that the same brutal human nature so evident in their own country exists as readily in the UK and, the movie implies, by extension the whole world. Violence, in other words, is not a product of place and circumstances but is rather a fundamental building block of human nature, existing in all of us, perhaps dormant in many, but needing only the right kind of provocation to manifest itself.What I liked most about Peckinpah's movie is that the violence in it made me feel bad. In revenge movies of today, we're asked to root for the protagonist who often becomes as sadistic as those who have done him wrong. The violence is exhilarating and titillating, and the more gruesome it is the better. But in "Straw Dogs," though we want to see Hoffman's character prevail against the sadists who attack his home, we're depressed by the degrading depths he's forced into in an effort to beat the bad guys at their own game. The moral is that when you're forced to become as bad as the bad guys, there are no longer any good guys left.This is a disturbing film that captures the restless anger and anxiety of a dark time in American culture.Grade: A</fullReview>

		<username>Scott LeBrun (Hey_Sweden)</username>
		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>An amazing movie, both intelligent and visceral.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>In addition to being one of the most controversial movies of its kind over 40 years ago, "Straw Dogs" is still quite a potent and upsetting experience in 2012, the kind of thing that not only has some cutting things to say about relationships and the nature of humankind but also affects us at a gut level as well. It doesn't flinch at any time, nor does it pussyfoot around. Quite simply, director / co-screenwriter Sam Peckinpah is unafraid at any time to show just how ugly things can get once they start escalating. A core theme is the time-honoured one of how much a "civilized" person can take before they snap and resort to primitive methods to protect their homes and lives. Based on the novel "The Siege of Trencher's Farm" by Gordon M. Williams, it tells the story of an American mathematician, David Sumner (Dustin Hoffman), who moves with his young wife Amy (English beauty Susan George) to an isolated village where tensions start arising between them and the locals, among them boisterous barfly Tom Hedden (Peter Vaughan) and Charlie Venner (Del Henney), with whom Amy was once involved. Things take a turn for the worst when the ostracized Henry Niles (played by an uncredited David Warner) is seen in the company of Hedden's daughter and the locals come to confront David, who's given Henry shelter after a car accident. As has been noted, Peckinpah doesn't need to concern himself with style as this story is compelling enough to not need a lot of embellishment. It's told in a methodical, straightforward fashion, with the pacing efficient but never quite racing, even in the brutal, climactic action, wherein David has to use his brains to battle his opponents. The cast is completely convincing right down the line, playing characters who are most definitely not one dimensional. The local atmosphere also helps immeasurably, in addition to Jerry Fielding's music. It makes sense that some viewers of this classic movie may not be able to really appreciate it as it is the sort of experience that sticks with you, for better and for worse. Its power is impossible to deny. The violence is strong and if one sees the uncut version with a key rape sequence restored, the impact is even stronger. "Straw Dogs" truly isn't for the faint of heart: it's grim, uncompromising, and does make one think about their own attitudes towards the subjects examined during the story. Top notch. 10 out of 10.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>"Smell a rat, see a rat, kill a rat".</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Back in the day, this was the movie that introduced me to Sam Peckinpah. Up till that time, I never really gave a thought to the importance of a director to a film, but this one changed my perspective. Peckinpah uses just as many subtle touches in his characterizations and story telling as he does for violence, and it's those nuances that bring a depth to the film. Consider the way David (Dustin Hoffman) wiped the blood off his hand after shooting the fowl, as if to wipe away the injustice of bringing down a helpless bird. Each success against his tormentors brought another round of cheers, culminating in that great bear trap maneuver. The set-up for that one was priceless, because all the while you're thinking someone's going to step in that thing, and suddenly it becomes Charlie's necktie.Because movie violence is such a staple today, the action here may seem tame by comparison, but just consider - Hoffman's character had to think on his feet and use the limited resources available to him to protect his home. No big guns or 'lock and load' action, just a man pushed to the limit deciding that "This is where I live. This is me". With that declaration, David crosses his own personal Rubicon and knows there will be no retreat or surrender, only victory or certain death in defeat.I'm watching the picture today as a bit of a warm up for the remake that comes out in a couple of weeks which I'm anxious to see. There will be that bias for the original but I'll go into it with an open mind. In the meantime, this is an all but unknown gem from the early Seventies, a time when film makers like Sam Peckinpah were pushing the envelope on sex, drugs, violence, and yes, even rape in a bold and forthright manner.</fullReview>

		<username>CountZero313</username>
		<score>0.6</score>
		<shortDescription>a morality play for the Vietnam generation</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Director Peckinpah and writer Goodman set out to shock in this uncompromising tale of brutality and vengeance in a small English village. A university mathematician and his flighty wife, an incongruous couple fleeing some undefined life-changing event, set up home in the rural hometown of his wife's childhood. The locals quickly set about terrorising them.Despite the 20th century setting, the template is medieval Morality Play. David Sumner (Dustin Hoffman) is the Righteous Man, husband to Amy (Susan George), the Fallen Woman. The band of yokels who brutalise them are less yobs and more Beelzabub and his acolytes. In realist terms, the story is implausible on many levels. David and Amy are an unlikely couple. One mass murderer in an English village would be rare, never mind a band of five of them. There is even a village idiot (read: pervert) whose continued survival amongst his barbarian neighbours is little short of miraculous. The explosion of violence at the end is telegraphed all the way through, and the outcome of that battle is a long shot, to say the least.Straw Dogs looks great with its vivid colour and strong framing. The acting is fantastic, with Susan George in particular asked to be defiant, vulnerable, clingy, murderous, sultry, lustful, disgusted, outraged, flirty... Apparently she was bullied on set, in order to push her to the extremes the role demands. It is not surprising news; the film threads through its misogyny lightly. The story is maddeningly perplexing, centering on the incongruities in David's character. He invites men who clearly lust after his wife into his home, and even chums along with them after they murder his cat. He is a victim then, except that when a vicar visits his home he becomes overbearing and vulgar. The lack of consistency is deliberate, as the filmmakers want you to leave with questions, not answers.However, the questions seem contrived, manufactured to puzzle only and not leading to any greater insight. The narrative thread of Straw Dogs is unconvincing, and the whole thing does not stand the test of time. The clunky exchange of dialogue that ends the film seems to suggest we are all lost souls. The message is undercut by being stated in an artificial world alien to our own. Part thriller, part adaptation of Paradise Lost, Straw Dogs seems to have had something to say to the Vietnam generation, but has little more than curiosity value now.</fullReview>

		<username>johnnyboyz</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Tough and unrelentingly bleak picture, as Peckinpah studies a clashing between classes and genders disturbingly playing out in a pent up and isolated locale.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The nausea settles in early on in Sam Peckinpah's Straw Dogs, an acute angle looking directly down at a town square from that of a church steeple; furthermore going on to inter-cut with another raw angle looking directly up at it from ground level encompassing a young girl perched on a gravestone. Death and religion greet us with these early compositions, and what follows is a masterclass in suspense and angst as classes clash; an unwinding and unravelling of pent up aggression as well as emotion between numerous parties set in the cold, grey, bleak zone of rural South West England. Peckinpah's film is effectively a series of setting things up before the consequent introducing of the proverbial item more than capable of knocking it back down; the placing of these two things in close proximity to one another the game the director takes glee in playing and executes masterfully.Dustin Hoffman plays David Sumner, a mathematician from America currently based in amidst the fields and dirt roads of an isolated English farmhouse with wife, who was once local to the area, Amy (George). David appears a man of little interest in conflict and seems to want to keep himself to himself as often as possible. Back at the house, some locals work on re-tiling the roof to the property's garage, in what is perceived as a lazy and deliberately slow manner by David; their exchanges carrying an unease from the very beginning through nerves to start with and only going on to maintain that sense of tension and disconnection. The true extent of the local's extended presence is later revealed to be more greatly linked to having the ability to be near Amy; the film coming to form, for all its parts, a constant back-and-forth between David and the locals culminating in a quite bloody finale with which the aforementioned nausea has well and truly settled in.The distinction between David and his garage working locals are evident on a basic level in the clothing either party wears and the vehicles they drive; Peckinpah inter-cutting how either of the two sides spend their evenings by showing us the time and activities the workers spend engaging in down at the local public house with David's trawling away in front of a blackboard, shutting himself away in from all else in the process, only to rifle through sums and equations. As the Sumner's occupy their secluded retreat, local teenagers look on from the boundaries of the property; seemingly fascinated by the nature of David and his presence, observing him as if a zoo animal in a pen ? he is very much an outsider to them all and ill-fitting to both their way of life and culture. The attraction those mending the roof of the garage have towards Amy is its own sordid, harrowing but brutally effective strand altogether; the stealing of some of her clothing and the establishing of her gradual sexual alienation from David sees her come to rather not mind being seen topless at a window during the day, everything boiling and brooding as one of her former partners from years ago remains around in town.The Sumner's marriage begins to hit its own contours in the road, the alienation they begin to feel from one another over David's work and the sexual frustration which appears to resonate within Amy a disturbing passage more greatly linked to that of Amy's presence back at the place from whence she comes; her joyous and carefree attitude to her situating there, with David, having her treat the whole thing as if some kind of holiday - much to her husband's gradual annoyance. It is to Hoffman's great credit that he is able to produce the timing and gradual steps in reaching a proverbial boiling point as the place and the people and the situation take their unremitting toll on him as he just tries to work. Pekinpah shoots most of the sequences as the palpable tension between both David and the locals rises from David's own point of view, the shooting of the workmen from a house window gradually having us come to observe the world through his own perspective having us side with the victimised party. A similar technique is applied later on, that of a perspective of one of the roofers during a duck hunt they invite him out on; the consequent seating of him in the middle of nowhere enabling them to identify him as someone totally ill at ease when given a weapon and put in a rather aggressive situation in which he must adopt the role of the aggressive perpetrator.The film will culminate with such information fresh in the minds of the roofers; a sickly skirmish involving David and the locals and like many of Peckinpah's finales, it consists of a shoot out or an all-out-war of some kind at a hemmed-in locale between numerous people harbouring different beliefs on the overall scenario thus forcing parties of numerous kinds to participate. Most of David's actions during the finale is born out of his own daft lust for heroism, the seeing of himself as a protector without knowing the full facts behind a situation; a blind rage of egotism and maniacal drive, the proving to himself and to others, specifically Amy, that he is not, in fact, at all cowardly. It's a notion which is ultimately demonised, mostly everybody comes off for the worse with nearly everybody loosing something ? some more serious than others. The film is brash and confrontational, but it requires Pekinpah's dab-hand at building displeasure, tension and an unnerved sense throughout in what is a grisly but eerily involving thriller in which classes violently come together.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>I keep crossing the long-awaited classics off my "to see-list" and for a Dustin Hoffman-ophile like myself, this one was long-awaited: Infamous thriller drama from Sam Peckinpah about timid American mathematician (Hoffman) who moves to rural England town with his British wife (George). Not before long they are slowly, but increasingly terrorized by townsmen who just as quickly shed their human morals and values. Takes it sweet time to build up (which is its major weakness) its atmosphere and detonate, but when it does, those final 40 min's will have you up the wall! Not only for its graphic violence (which by today's standards seems fairly average!), but the bravura suspense trip that is nothing but a pure editing triumph. One can only guess how much John Carpenter was influenced by that house barricade before making "Assault on precinct 13"! Given its reputation, this is definitely not one-track minded, but intensely forceful and more than ambiguous, including a rape scene wide open for discussion just as much as its classic central violence- and self-defense theme that is subtly implied more than once throughout: How much would YOU take before hitting back? Also, small, good characterizations (mostly by not making Hoffmann's &amp; George's relationship all lovey-dovey) and shot with director Peckinpah's distinct flamboyant flair. 6 out of 10 from Ozjeppe</fullReview>

Gradually, the tension builds in this movie, advancing mercilessly towards the climax that cannot be averted. The music is fitting. There is infrequent moderate language, strong, brutal disturbing and controversial content and violence in this. The DVD I got held only three trailers: Eddie, The Big Easy and Albino Alligator. I recommend this to any fan of the director, the genre, and/or to a lesser extent, anyone else involved in making it. 8/10</fullReview>

		<fullReview>In Peckinpah's Cornish western, there's a suggestion the co-writer/director may not be taking things too seriously. During the course of the Siege of Trencher's Farm, the odious rat catcher (Jim Norton) is seen riding a kid's tricycle. One of his fellow thugs chases him with it, threatening to wrap it round his neck. The next shot shows them racing each other, both on tricycles, amidst the mayhem. I can't think of any other slapstick moments in Peckinpah's other violent set pieces. And later, Hoffman dispatches the rat catcher with what looks suspiciously like a golf swing. Perhaps it would have been more fitting to the latter's profession if he'd been the one to succumb to the trap.With neat irony, Hoffman's maths professor has picked the wrong fight in protecting David Warner, who has just (albeit accidentally) killed village strumpet Sally Thomsett (who I suspect of killing the cat). At the moment when he fears his wife Susan George will switch sides to old beau Charlie (Del Henney), he specifically becomes his rival, striking her, then pulling her by the hair, both of which Charlie has inflicted on her prior to the bizarre rape turned love scene turned gang rape. So whilst there's audience pleasure in seeing the bad guys wiped out, are we also to acknowledge this as an anti-violence statement?One thing's for sure: this mismatched couple isn't going to make it.Along with those mentioned above, Ken Hutchison is also impressive as the 'bad' rapist, in a uniformly excellent cast.Interesting to see the name of Tony Lawson as one of the editors. There's occasional time jump editing in this that figures strongly in his work for Nic Roeg (from Bad Timing onwards), and also in Peckinpah's later Cross of Iron, which along with Barry Lyndon Lawson also edited. And, I don't know if it's just me, but I love John Coquillon's grey skies!</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Traumatic</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Vicious, compelling, manipulative, obscene, cathartic and confrontational-how many 30 year old films still fuel debate and split opinion as fiercely as the infamous rape and revenge triller Straw Dogs? Its very well made, very well done, and very nasty. Its the last aftershock from a seismic golden age in Seventies cinema-A Clockwork Orange, The Exorcist and few others which were also the last to be granted certificate after almost 20 years in censorship limbo. Despite its powerful merits as a work of art, Straw dogs remains probably the most notorious mainstream film ever made on British soil. While emotionally powerful in a very negative way and also profoundly fatalistic, its also frighteningly soul shatteringly bleak.</fullReview>

		<username>(dj_bassett)</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Alternately brilliant and frustrating</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Dustin Hoffman and Susan George move to rural England and come into conflict with a gang of local hooligans (anyone who's lived in small towns/rural environments will recognize these types immediately). The situation escalates until finally, through an odd confluence of events, Hoffman takes a stand. Frustrating movie is alternately exceptionally brilliant and ridiculously contrived, sometimes within a few seconds of each other. The plot is incredibly contrived and unbelievable the moment you stop to think about it (who really believes Hoffman and George would ever really end up together, for instance?) , but individual sequences: Susan George's rape, the big standoff at the farm, David Warner killing the girl, the final shot, etc. are immensely well-done. One has the feeling that this, like WILD BUNCH, was an attempt on Peckinpah's part to adapt a genre tale to his own ends; unfortunately, the restrictions of the thriller genre (which STRAW DOGS essentially is) confine Peckinpah in the way a Western never could. Flawed, then, but still well worth watching ? a powerful statement of a powerful vision.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>Nothing raises the ire of an alpha-male more than seeing a hot chick with a nerd. And that is just what we have with Straw Dogs. Dustin Hoffman plays a nerd who is married to a beautiful young English woman. The two have moved to a tiny town in rural England to escape the turmoil of the good old USA and so Hoffman can write some kind of book about math or science, or something nerdy like that. The couple barely gets settled into their new home before the locals begin harassing them.Hoffman is not a target simply because he is a foreigner. He is targeted because he seems out of place in the local town, especially in the pub. Early on we see him dare to enter the establishment and ask for "any kind of American cigarettes". He also commits a great sin by walking in front of a guy throwing darts. But his greatest sin is the fact that he is married to a former girlfriend of one of the local alpha-males. This only leads to jealousy, and considerable acrimony.A few of the guys are very slowly helping to upgrade the young couple's home. At one point, the young woman dares to tease the locals with a quick glimpse of her bare breasts. Before you know it, the harassment is under way. A pair of her panties are stolen for starters. Then, their cat is hanged in the closet. (Poor cat! First we see Hoffman throwing fruit at it, then it gets killed!) Eventually, the young woman is brutally raped. Things come to a head when the couple give shelter to a wounded man suspected of murdering a girl back in town. Things are finally solved by a very violent confrontation. We see Hoffman's character change from an even-tempered pacifist to a violent vigilante after he is pushed too far.This film is actually quite good when you add up all the elements. The film looks great, even though the director likes to use a few too many cuts for my taste. There is one hell of a lot of glass that gets broken. I haven't seen a film break so much glass since Another 48 Hours. Is it some kind of coincidence that the writer for that film (Roger Spotiswoode) was the editor of this film? The violence is raw, but not over the top as some would claim. There is a graphic rape scene, but it's nothing near as bad as the Death Wish movies or Casualties of War. The film is very well-acted, and the conclusion should satisfy many viewers.This is not a landmark piece of cinema, but it certainly will be worth your time to take a look at it. The unrated version is the only one I have seen, and it would be about 8 of 10 stars.The Hound.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>"Straw Dogs" (1971): Sam Peckinpah wrote and directed this late peace movement era film, which flew in the face of his entire audience (as usual). With stars like Dustin Hoffman and Susan George, it could not be dismissed as just another "B grade" movie. Even Peckinpah's production values were much improved. It's a simple story: a young couple moves to the English countryside to find a little peace and quiet, pursue intellectual interests, work on their relationship, and breathe some fresh air?but find just the reverse waiting for them. Hoffman, a bookish, wimpy sort of a fellow, and his wife, an immature girl-woman who has no clue who she is or what she wants from a man, are increasingly subjected not only to one another, but to the "locals". Peckinpah is amazingly patient with "Straw Dogs" ? normally known for his relentless "ballets in blood" ? takes it's time building and building the tension, until there is nothing left to do but what must be done. Hoffman's character, David, has the spotlight throughout, however George's role of "Amy", gets increasingly impressive. Most interesting to me was the emotional &amp; spiritual journey of David, whose reactions to what must be done are NOT what he, his wife, or we expected. The very last scene, as he drives a mildly retarded man home in the night, is haunting and unforgettable.</fullReview>

		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Interesting and frustrating in equal measures</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Sam Peckinpah's Cornish western was undoubtedly groundbreaking and provocative thirty years ago, but time hasn't been kind to it. Too many questions remain unanswered, credibility is stretched far beyond breaking point (regularly) and virtually every plot point is telegraphed well in advance. Having said that, it remains a fascinating piece of cinema, with top-notch photography, direction and (especially) editing, and authentic performances all round. By the time you get to the frantic, disturbing finale, all your sympathies lie with the Hoffman character, which is either a good or a bad thing depending on your viewpoint. Peckinpah pulls out every trick in the book to manipulate the audience into demanding blood and thunder, and then delivers it in spades. My advice to anyone who wants to watch Straw Dogs is to choose a time and a place carefully, as this still isn't a film to be taken lightly. Ultimately, it's a matter of taste, and very much a product of its time, both of which should be taken into account.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Retains the power to shock and disturb.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>A mild mannered American Mathematition and his English wife move into her remote Cornish farmhouse where she grew up. The locals resent his presence and the fact that he has married one of 'their girls'. While driving home one foggy night, the couple hit a local retarded man who has accidentally suffocated a girl from the village. Not aware of this, they take him to the farmhouse for help. Before long the local roughnecks are laying seige to the house, demanding that the couple turn the man over to them for 'questioning' about the girl's dissappearance. The American refuses, knowing what will happen if he hands over the suspect, and the situation descends into all out violence. I watched this last night as it had its UK television premiere on Channel Four. Along with 'A Clockwork Orange' it was banned for many years here, only seeing the light of day on DVD late last year. The previous evening Channel Four screened an excellent documentary on the troubled history of the film, presented by Film Critic Mark Kermode. My appetite duly whetted, I sat down expecting either a masterpiece or a total letdown. The result lay somewhere between the two. The film builds slowly and menacingly, before finally exploding into violence near the end. However, so accustomed have we become to graphic on screen violence that its effect is lessened compared to what it would have been 32 years ago. The transformation of Dustin Hoffmann's character remains interesting nevertheless. The real problem with the film is of course the attitude to Susan George's character. From the outset Peckinpah depicts her as a tease and a flirt, so by the time we get to the double rape we are almost supposed to think 'she was asking for it'. This is compounded by her reaction to the first rape. These are dangerous messages for filmmakers to send out to society and one must question Peckinpah's agenda here. At his best (The Wild Bunch, Cross Of Iron) Sam Peckinpah was a truly gifted Director. 'Straw Dogs' is no classic but it remains interesting and challenging. I'm glad I took the time to see it and I would probably watch it again. However, it can be an unsettling veiwing experience, but perhaps that was the point, that violence is distressing and ugly.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Microscope on the connection of violence and sex</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I cannot think of another movie that explores the nature of violence better than Straw Dogs.Peckinpah manages to dream up ideal situations where his male characters' sense of masculinity is so threatened, that the only obvious response is an explosion of violence. This applies not only to the bourgeoisie American protagonist David (Dustin Hoffman) but also to the proletariat brits that lay siege on his house.However, "Straw Dogs" gives the female protagonist, Amy (Susan George) the most sympathetic light. It is true that female sexuality is often the spark that sets off the violence in the movie, but the fuel is fed by frustrated, confused and misogynistic males.The tension in the movie starts tight and does not let up until after the final bloody climax. Movies rarely achieve such palpable violence and force the audience to think about many dark topics. If the movie is ugly and difficult to watch, it is because Peckinpah achieve exactly what he wanted.</fullReview>

		<username>TheVid</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>This remarkable film is Peckinpah's definitive exploration the so-called "male rite of passage".</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Peckinpah comes full force with STRAW DOGS, after various examinations of male-bonding and macho rituals in excellent films like THE WILD BUNCH and THE FAR COUNTRY. Here Peckinpah and his writer, David Zelig Goodman, do whatever it takes to set us up for the full impact of their protagonist's trial by fire. That the mechanisms and characterizations employed are controversial is a moot point; that they deliver the intended emotional impact, is the ultimate measure of the picture's power. Themes and actions here are multi-layered, ambivalent and intense, as they often are in truly powerful works by major filmmaking talents. STRAW DOGS is an evocative, highly personal, philosophical release by a passionate filmmaker with strong opinions and unrestrained sensibilities. Sam Peckinpah's masterpiece is now a worthy addition to The Criterion Collection in a nicely packaged special-edition DVD.</fullReview>

At the end, he says to Niles that he too doesn't know his way home, so he has acknowledged his fate. This is a tussle with himself, his masculinity and his role as a man. A mathematician who is loathe to use violence overcomes the odds when the chips are down, to prove to himself he can do it. The English cast are brilliantly sinister and uncompromising in their attitudes towards Hoffman. All round a good film. **** out of *****</fullReview>

		<date>2000-11-25</date>
		<username>pzanardo (pzanardo@math.unipd.it)</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>A controversial but powerful movie</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Speaking of "Straw Dogs", let me paraphrase Oscar Wilde: a movie is not either sexist or anti-sexist: a movie is either good or bad, and that's all. "Straw Dogs" is certainly a very good movie, though not reaching the level of the major Peckinpah's achievements.Perhaps it is better to point out first some defects of the movie. It is too long and slow-paced, and after a while the viewer just starts to wait for the (predictable) final show-down. The acting is somewhat flat, and Hoffmann seems uneasy in the role of David Sumner. David's wife Amy (Susan George) was born in the village; the mentality of small communities makes it unlikely that the local thugs torment so ruthlessly the young couple (even the rape seems more probable that this continuous teasing). There is a major naiveness: a mathematician like David could not live in such an isolated village. He should need scientific contacts with colleagues, or at least a library to consult journals (books are not enough). A mathematician is not a writer who, a priori, could live like a hermit.The movie have remarkable merits. The setting is much accurate. The sense of strain and suspense is masterly built. The final fight is fantastic, as always when Peckinpah deals with action.But what makes "Straw Dogs" really outstanding are two ideas of the author, in fact the two most original but also disturbing and controversial ones. First, in the rape scene, when Amy allures her former boy-friend and seems to appreciate his rape (but she doesn't realize that he is not alone!). And later she says nothing to her husband. Most disturbing! We all would have preferred for Amy to be an innocent victim. But Peckinpah likes tough folks: men have sexual desires, and women have sexual desires, too. When something goes wrong, Peckinpah's men don't keep whining and seeking help; and the same for women. Is this sort of symmetry man-woman sexism? Maybe, but in any case the audience is shaken to the core, and this was probably the purpose of the director.The second extremely original idea is that the final slaughter is not caused by the rape (which David ignores, don't forget it). In fact David becomes dreadfully violent on the ground of his liberal principles (!), to save the idiot of the village Henry from a lynch-law. And here Amy shows her meanness, wanting to abandon the miserable Henry to the assassins (that also are her own rapists). And then we see David dizzy at the revelation of his own capacity to be violent: this is a powerful piece of cinema.It is difficult to really love "Straw Dogs"; probably it is easier to hate it, in spite of its merits. Nonetheless, the movie is a powerful work, with plenty of extremely interesting if disturbing matter. After all, a must-see film (not for delicate souls).</fullReview>
	</review>

		<fullReview>Sam Peckinpah had that rare ability to generate unforgettable images onto the screen--and eventually into your deep memory. Straw Dogs is filled with these sharp, vivid and unusual images. No matter how many times I view the film, I still can feel the wet and ghostly fog that engulfs the killing field. And I am always stunned by that strange smirk on Dustin Hoffman's face as he goes about destroying his murderous tormentors. And how about the shot of the good constable killed by a shotgun blast and sent flying across the yard. And in almost revenge for this act, there is the horrible and painful quick shot of the deflected shotgun blast tearing off the shoe and foot of the old man coming through the window. And the vivid images go on and on: Dustin Hoffman hurling hot oil in the faces of his attackers... beating the red-nosed clown to death ...and killing one of them by pushing his head into a beartrap And throughout the rampage there is the deep etched face of Hoffman with his broken glasses and determined stance. I recently caught this 30-year-old masterpiece on cable--and I can't wait to see it again!</fullReview>

Sam Peckinpah was a master at building suspense and then capping it off with well orchestrated yet graphic violence. What separates "Straw Dogs" from Kuberick's film is the personalization of the story and violence. While Kuberick's work takes almost a pedestrian view of violence, Peckinpah draws us into the story. The result is a disturbing, yet masterful character study of violence, and the lengths a person will go to defend what is his.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>STRAW DOGS is yet another incredible work from the late Sam Peckinpah. His uncanny ability to totally absorb you into the story prevails once again as it did in the WILD BUNCH. All I ever read these days about this film is controversy &amp; of course, violence. Dustin Hoffman gives a superb performance as a mathematician who moves to England with his new wife. They return to her maiden village, where trouble with the locals seems inevitable.My father comes from a small village in Portugal, and speaking from experience, this film is totally believable &amp; realistic. At the end of the film Doctor Sumner empathizes with Henry Niles when he says, "I don't know my way home". At this point in the film Dr Sumner feels as alienated as a known petaphile.And finally, my only problem with the film: What was up with Amy from the point of the rape to the end of the film? At first she fights the rape then enjoys it? And at the very end of the film, she wants to leave the protection &amp; sanctity of a man who would die for her, to go outside the house where a bunch of dirty rapists wait for her? Why did she stay behind in the house with all those corpses and not leave with David? Why was there no resolution to David &amp; Amy's relationship?But I don't blame these questions I have on Peckinpah, these are the fault of the writer. And I have to admit they left me confused, angry, and bewildered. So these questions are the reason I refer to this film as a slightly flawed masterpiece at best. Sam Peckinpah, one of the greatest, most innovative filmmakers of his time; only matched by Stanley Kubrick &amp; his present-day prodigy, John Woo.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>So far there has been no film in cinema history that manages to make you detest violence as much as this film. 		<username>Steffi_P</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>"Shoot with us sometime"</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>*Some vague spoilers* Film censors, among their many sins, have a tendency to get the wrong end of the stick. Straw Dogs became one of the most high profile victims of censorship on the grounds of its sheer brutal violence (not to mention an especially grotesque rape scene). But despite its content it was never intended to be a brainless bloodfest. The aim was to be shocking, but only for the purpose of condemning violence, director and co-writer Sam Peckinpah's belief being that an unpleasant enough depiction of something could serve to ward people off it.Straw Dogs has also been defended, quite rightly, for its overall quality and intelligence in production. Let's focus first of all on Peckinpah's work as a director. His style is so full of noticeable intricacies that almost every shot has been picked apart by his gaggle of devotees. However I find while his fans tend to marvel at his symbolism, foreshadowing and motifs, they miss his strong command of basic film grammar. Take, for example, the shot near the beginning where Del Henney (Charlie) helps Dustin Hoffman get the mantrap in the back of the car, which Peckinpah fanatics will point out as foreshadowing the later physical struggle between the two men. But more important to how this scene impacts upon us is the fact that Hoffman appears cut off from his wife, and is unable to protect her from Henney's advances. Most in the audience will not be aware of this ? we are not supposed to be ? but it does give us a very strong impression on a subconscious level, far more than any bit of "symbolism". Throughout the picture Peckinpah is using the same kind of tricks to show us who is dominant, who is isolated, who is in trouble and so forth.Peckinpah's analysts also tend to overlook the acting in his pictures, which is a pity because he invariably worked with excellent albeit unsung players. Many were considered for the lead role, including Donald Sutherland and Jack Nicholson, both of whom have the necessary capacity for mad rage, but only Dustin Hoffman has the meekness that makes it seem such a case of a breaking point reached. He also keeps this mask of calm normality regardless of what is happening, which is highly effective. Susan George is pretty good too for someone who made her name in sleazy B-flicks, and gives a very believable presentation of a woman on the cusp between teens and maturity. Best of all however are the men of the village. Peter Vaughan is a bit of spot-on casting, genuinely scary as the boisterous patriarch. The others, Henney, Ken Hutchison, Jim Norton and Donald Webster give such incredibly real portrayals of young English lads. I have met men like that, and have suffered their attentions.Which brings us round to that controversial rape scene which while nowhere near as graphic as the violence is nevertheless exceedingly unpleasant in what it implies. The rape was not in the novel, so why was it included for the screen adaptation? It has an unusual bearing on the story and our reading of the final outcome. We the audience witness the rape and thus have more cause to see the village lads as detestable and deserving of their fate. Hoffman's character however remains unaware of it, which means his violent retribution is purely on grounds of defending Niles and his home. But the fact we know that two of these guys are rapists makes the violence seem righteous, even satisfactory to an audience. Couple this with the thrilling notion that Sumner is using his knowledge and wits to defeat their brute strength, as well as Peckinpah's knack for making action scenes so wonderfully dynamic, and the violence of Straw Dogs actually becomes exciting and enjoyable.Does this then mean that the film censors were right? In fact, both Peckinpah and the censors were wrong. Straw Dogs will not scare people off violence, nor will it attract them to it. We pick up our morality from real life, not from a cinema screen. Straw Dogs is not an effective message movie, and it is not a dangerous one. It is, quite simply, a decent work of entertainment. Enjoy it as one.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<fullReview>I recently bought Straw Dogs on DVD, (the uncut version) having never seen it before. I've seen some pretty uncompromising and raw movies before, but this surely is one of its kind - brutal, controversial, hard as nails, and ultimately brilliant. The story is in paper a simple one - mathematician David (Hoffman) a shy American moves to rural Cornwall with his wife (Susan George), to escape the big city, and all it entails, all the strife that big cities give you. So they move to the countryside... only it becomes obvious to them and us that the country ain't so different from the city after all. Directed by Sam Peckinpah,(the wild bunch), and with Hoffman and George riveting, its a tale of violence repressed, that which is within all of us, what many of us hide... Its a harsh gritty unflinching piece that probably due to its content (several scenes will blow you away) has never got the credit it deserves, but folks there isn't anything like this at all in cinema - a genuine masterpiece of wrought iron tension. 10/10 David Ford</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>You Don't Come In Here</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>David Sumner is an American mathematician living with his wife Amy in an isolated English village. David struggles to fit in with the locals, one of whom is an old boyfriend of Amy's. When David shelters a retarded man who has attacked a girl, five men try to smash their way in. However, perhaps they have underestimated the quiet man ?Straw Dogs is the movie for anyone who thinks violence is in any way cool, glamorous or fun, because it presents it the way it should be; ugly, stupid and demeaning for all involved. That's not to say the movie is any of those things - it's not perfect, but it's an engrossing story, a brilliantly edited mixture of intriguing themes, and the last half-hour is one of the most nail-biting climaxes to any movie. The ideas are fascinating; why can't the locals accept David, why is Amy so childish, why does David feel obliged to protect Henry, where does David's resolve come from ? Peckinpah depicts these with brilliant visual motifs, like the contrast of the notorious rape scene with David shooting the bird on the moor. As with all his work, there's a poeticism to his images which transcends the vulgarity and the violence in the stories he tells. If you don't believe me, compare it with any of his many imitators (e.g. Michael Winner's Death Wish), which are often just nasty for the sake of it and don't take any time to explore the nature of violence. None of the cast are really very likable in this movie but everyone plays it well; George is pretty unforgettable in the controversial role of a lifetime, Hutchison is one of the scariest tough guys in British cinema (check him out also in Ladyhawke) and the ever-reliable Warner appears unbilled in the Lenny role. Written by David Zelag Goodman and Peckinpah, based on the novel The Siege Of Trencher's Farm, by Gordon M. Williams. This was an enforced but productive change of pace for Peckinpah, shot in Cornwall in south-west England, with an all-British cast and crew (except Hoffman), featuring great camera-work by John Coquillon. This is one of several movies right-wing institutions like to defame and ban, which is reason enough to see it, but it's really a fine example of the pressure-cooker plot which build up to an explosive finale, something cinema does very well when handled right. See it and think about it.</fullReview>

The other main propellant is her self-righteous husband, David, who's concern with other people goes about as far as his glasses do.As the story unfolds and the dynamics of the characters are played out, we see one situation after another that is couped by the different character's pettiness and egotism. It all culminates in a needlessly violent fight between the locals, who ostensibly are looking for justice, and David who ostensibly is standing up for what he sees as right.There is however no justice or nobility anywhere near this culmination of events. The characters are using these things as excuses for playing out their own petty and selfish agendas. The motivations and tensions have been built up masterfully by Peckinpah throughout the film and ends in a tempest of dark, unrelenting and violent irony.Many people will say that this is a pessimistic film that takes a needlessly dark view of how humans behave. Well, that could be true, but sometimes... that is what you must do.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Peckinpah's tribute to Hitchcock</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Why have so many viewers found this film so offensive? I strongly suggest viewing Hitchcock's's early British films (esp. Blackmail) before seeing this one.</fullReview>
