		<username>monkeeking</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>"Straw Dogs" is still an enjoyable and thought provoking film.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>WARNING: LARGE PART OF PLOT Reveled Director Sam Peckinpah's "The Wild Bunch" (1969) was one of the most controversial films ever made for its time and is still be considered violent today. Plot progression was dealt with quickly but the violence was carefully elongated to create full pitched battles with shocking blood letting. Most of the violence was filmed in slow motion, even when civilians were caught up in it. Death was no longer reserved for the "bad-guys" dressed in black. Every character was portrayed in different shades of gray. Also, Peckinpah used great dialogue and characterization so that combined with the performances of excellent actors (William Holden, Ernest Borgnine, Warren Oates, Ben Johnson, Edmond O'Brien, Robert Ryan) he managed to pull together not a mindless gore-fest, but a picture that spoke of loyalty between friends and honor in death. It was one of the greatest movies of all time.Two years later Peckinpah directed "Straw Dogs". Although it, too, can be considered violent and it was definitely controversial (banned in the UK), "Straw Dogs" is a slight disappointment. It's set in a small town in rural England which becomes the new home of American David Sumner (Dustin Hoffman) and his British wife Amy (Susan George). Together they are trying to escape the violence of American society and decide to settle down in Amy's peaceful hometown where David can find the time he needs to write his book on "stellar interiors and their radiation characteristics". The details on what he does are very vague. All that Peckinpah wants us to know is that David is one of society's intellectual elite. Of course, to follow the cliché when it comes to nerds in thrillers, David is portrayed as a coward without a spine. The locals soon begin to dislike David since his mannerisms seem alien to them. The situation deteriorates faster because one of the locals, Charlie Venner (Del Henney) was once Amy's boyfriend and is obviously jealous of David.The Sumner's move into Amy's father's old house and hire several men to repair the roof on their garage. Slowly the men (Charlie among them) begin to harass the two and invade their privacy. It gets worse as their actions become more sadistic and abusive. The tensions finally break one night when David is trying to protect the village idiot from an angry mob. Janice, the daughter of an uncontrollable drunk (also related to Charlie), was seen walking home one night with the idiot (who has a shady past when it comes to the safety of young girls) but then disappears. David, while driving home from a community event, accidentally hits the idiot and finds that it's his responsibility to take care of him at home. The drunk gathers together a mob and tries to manhandle the idiot into telling him where Janice is. David sums up his courage and decides to be a man, taking on a "my house is my castle" mentality. A bloody siege ensues.Similar to "The Wild Bunch", "Straw Dogs" has many of Peckinpah's trademarks. It opens to a scene of children torturing a dog ("The Wild Bunch" opened to the same thing except the dog was a scorpion). Both films end in bloody climaxes. The script for "Straw Dogs" has well developed characters (right up to the last twenty minutes) and there is an excellently created atmosphere that helps deliver a sense of uneasiness with each line delivered by the outstanding cast. The differences between Amy and David, along with the harassment of the locals, all creates an almost unbearable tension. Amy is a lighthearted and beautiful young woman who acts so immature it's childish, and David is a selfish, solitary workaholic afraid of taking risks. The surrounding characters are all stupid, rude farmers with the exception of the local magistrate. All is perfectly set for the climax. Actually, the setup is so simple it's a little predictable. From there the movie goes further downhill. Peckinpah showed his talent for directing violent climaxes in "The Wild Bunch", but in "Straw Dogs" the violence is so long it's silly. The mob circles the Sumner home trying to break in. The doors are thick oak reinforced with steal, so it would seem sensible to go through the windows. No, that would shorten the action so they mill about for a while destroying the property. All is plausible since they're drunk, but a minute before they were sober and on a mission to save a young girl. Plausibility comes into question. It's all for violence's sake, which in the process cheapens the movie.Besides these flaws "Straw Dogs" is an enjoyable motion picture with a different message than that of "The Wild Bunch's". In the latter, criminals past their prime go down with guns blazing to save their friend, proving their honor amongst thieves. It portrayed the "bad guys" as honorable but the "good guys" as greedy railroad barons. It showed that no man can be classified as either "black" or "white". We're all gray and everyone has sinned, it just matters whose shoes you're in whose eyes you see things through. "Straw Dogs", however, is about one man's personal transformation in order to protect his abused dignity. As the tag-line says, "The knock at the door meant the birth of one man and the death of seven others." "Straw Dogs" is still an enjoyable and thought provoking film. 8/10</fullReview>
	</review>

		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>Acquired Taste Territory</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Appreciation of this movie seems to depend on just how far you're willing to stretch a metaphor.It is possible, for example, to read the character of David as a representation of liberal American idealism; an educated professional, a bit of a geek, something of a social outcast, who while supporting the student protests sweeping the country, doesn't have the balls to actively join them. He witnesses atrocities like the killings at Kent State but doesn't want to get involved. As a result, he flees the country in fear and disgust to a foreign rural idyll where he hopes that he can live his life quietly with his head in the sand (or in a book) and never be forced to actually stand up and fight for something.David's English wife, Amy, represents the loose living hedonism of the period; an individual who on the surface is all rebellious and anti-authoritarian, the kind of person who would happily jump in to join the protesters just to shock her family and friends, but would be the first to give in and run crying for help when the sh*t really hits the fan.And so you have these two people ? desperate and self-delusional - holed up in a ramshackle Cornish farmhouse trying to pretend that nothing in the world exists, let alone matters. When they are besieged by the forces or irrational, howling hatred, they are forced to make a decision; capitulate or fight.The entire movie, therefore, becomes a metaphor of Nixon-era social-politics. A savage indictment not only of bigotry and violence, but of the liberal pacifist refusal to combat such evil.The script, however, is often awkward, the supporting actors erratic at best, the cinematography is bland, and the editing has all the choppy hallmarks of inexperience. The lead performances from Hoffman and George are exceptional, though, with incredible range, complexity and subtlety. Not a single moment passes by when their faces aren't a turmoil of contradicting emotions and motivations, and it's a mystery why neither received ANY award recognition.Overall, 'Straw Dogs' is another fascinating, albeit nihilistic, insight into the paranoid, fearful, angry and injured American psyche of the early seventies.Be warned: Those viewers who are unable to stretch the metaphor to such lengths, will find the entire movie an ugly, senseless and depressing exercise in tedium.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Thought-provoking study of violence and machismo</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>6/10CONTAINS SPOILERSHoffman contributes one of his finest performances and Peckinpah provides his usual intelligent direction. The violence is shocking but not as graphic as Kubrik's "A Clockwork Orange" from the same year (1971). Possibly the main reason that the film caused so much offence on its release (it was subsequently banned in the UK for 25 years) is its neolithic sexual politics - particularly the ambiguous "rape" scene.Hoffman plays David Sumner, a quiet and passive mathematician locked in an intricate power struggle with his English wife Amy (Susan George). The couple have recently moved to the remote Cornish village where Amy was brought up, ironically to escape the potential for violence in America. During the first half of the film, we see the couple playing a series of chess games, which Peckinpah uses to symbolize the constant mind-games the couple are playing. Although playful, each "player" is intent on gaining the upper hand, with Amy seemingly winning each time.David also has to contend with a group of local villagers who seem intent on making his life miserable and are responsible for a series of unpleasant acts designed to unsettle him. Perhaps the worst thing for David is that his wife seems to encourage the attentions of the group when they start leering at her. By the end of the film, the relentless provocation from all sides finally pushes David to breaking point as he embarks on an unforgettable spree of violence.Sumner's transition from mild-mannered husband to ruthless murderer is sudden rather than gradual. His explosive outburst is simply the result of not being able to stand the frustration and humiliation welling up inside him any longer. Peckinpah's triumph is his manipulation of the audience to such an extent that even the most refined viewer is acquiesced to rooting for David as he is reduced to behaving in a way that goes against everything he (and a civilized society) stands for. No matter how much we think we have progressed, the primal instinct of violence is still buried somewhere inside every man.The film is generally well-acted and Jerry Fielding was nominated for an Oscar for a compelling score. However, Straw Dogs is let down by a slow beginning and the lengthy final scene may be a bit too gratuitous for some people's tastes.</fullReview>

You did a good thing for a bad guy." I know if I were in Hoffman's shoes, I would've handed the man over and let him die. As long as I'm not the one putting a bullet in his head, I'm no murderer. Hell, he could've saved himself the agony of having these lowlifes pulverize his house if he only let the man go. His wife is obviously in intense disagreement with him. Though I don't agree with Hoffman's decision, it presented an interesting "What if" question for the audience to answer. I haven't seen any of Peckinpah's other works, so I have no grounds for comparison, but it's definitely an intense film, even for today's standards. It's just not a film for the squeamish. As well as some disturbing rape scenes, there are flashes of graphic violence towards the end. My score: 7 (out of 10)</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>A robust and brutal masterpiece</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Loved and hated in equal measures and banned for almost twenty years because of a brutal rape scene; you can say what you want about Sam Peckinpah's violent thriller Straw Dogs, but undeniably the film is very powerful. Sam Peckinpah made The Wild Bunch in 1969; a film that has gone down in history as one of the most violent westerns ever made. But while that film shown the violence head on; Straw Dogs broods and makes the audience think before delivering its shocks; and the result is much more powerful, as not only will you be shocked by what you're seeing on screen but you will be forced to think about it also. The film is based on a novel by Gordon Williams and focuses on David and Amy Sumner. He's an American mathematician and she's a former country girl; and together they move to the Cornish countryside so he can concentrate on his latest book. Upon arriving they are given a cold reception from their new neighbours. However, the frostiness soon turns sour as the locals turn to physical violence directed at both the man and his wife.Dustin Hoffman takes the lead role and is absolutely perfect as the quaint American trying to ignore his troubles. Hoffman has given a number of great performances in his career; but for me this is the best. There's never ant doubt that he is the character he is playing and the film would not be anywhere near as good without him in it. The perky Susan George is his opposite number and performs well in a role that includes some unpleasant scenes. The atmosphere that Peckinpah creates in the small English town is completely overpowering and it's very easy to completely understand the fear felt by the lead characters. The film is famous for its rape scene and while it's not as visceral as the ones seen in some other films; it's extremely unpleasant and Peckinpah manages to force the audience into deciding whether or not the victim actually 'deserved' it, which is far more shocking than anything we actually see on screen. The film is famous for its violence; but the violence actually doesn't feature strongly until the conclusion. By then, we are completely at ease with the characters and the situation - which makes it all the more shocking. The final forty minutes of this film are among the most tense I've ever seen and ensures the film ends on a pulsating high. The central characters are the most important thing about it, however, and the film doesn't delight in its violence; it's simply there as a means to an end in the development of the lead character. Overall, Straw Dogs is a violent masterpiece and a film that has to be seen by anyone who considers themselves a film fan.</fullReview>

		<date>2008-06-29</date>
		<username>Hancock_the_Superb</username>
		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Powerful, intense meditation on the allure and repulsion of violence</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>American astrophysicist David Sumner (Dustin Hoffman) is a wimpy pacifist who grows tired of the strife and violence raging across America. Along with his pretty young wife, Amy (Susan George), he travels to George's hometown in Cornwall, England. Sumner quickly finds that beneath the idyllic scenery and veneer of small-town friendliness lie a clannish society based on violence and exclusion-ism. A group of townspeople begin playing a series of increasingly vicious pranks on David and Amy, culminating in Amy's rape by two of the more repulsive townspeople (Del Henney and Ken Hutchison). Things still continue on, until David takes in the village idiot, Harry Niles (David Warner), after hitting him with a car and tries to save him from a mob who saw him abduct a young girl. David tries to sort the situation out peacefully, but before long he finds himself resorting to violence to defend himself and his home."Straw Dogs" is an amazingly powerful film, and a widely misunderstood one as well. Critics who seem unable to analyze films on anything but surface meaning accuse it of glamorizing sadism and violence; feminists harp on the film's rape scene and the portrayal of Amy, denouncing the film as a chauvinist fantasy. Both readings of the film are wholly off-base. Although perhaps not as deep as some of Sam Peckinpah's other works, it's easily his best shy of "The Wild Bunch", and deals with a deep (and disturbing) topic: humanity's lust for and glorification of violence and death.Say the name Sam Peckinpah to anyone and what comes to mind? In pretty much every instance, the answer is violence. Graphic violence, slow-motion shoot-outs with bright red blood spurting out of bullet wounds. This is a simplistic way to look at Peckinpah; in his best work (Ride the High Country, The Wild Bunch) he created deep themes and well-rounded characters worthy of a classic novel or play. Even some of his weaker efforts, like Pat Garrett and Billy the Kid and Cross of Iron, have interesting ideas to present, even if not entirely successful. But, it is certainly true that violence - its effects, its terror, its place in society, and, most of all, social enjoyment of it - is a central theme of Peckinpah's films. Merely judging "Straw Dogs" on the fact that it IS violent is childish and simplistic; it's much more fruitful to address WHY Peckinpah opted to make it violent, and what he's trying to say."Straw Dogs" is perhaps the ultimate distillation of Peckinpah's views on violence. Peckinpah was an ardent fan of Robert Ardrey, the playwright-anthropologist who hypothesized that humans are inured to violence by instinctual urges rather than social pressure or upbringing. This is a very hard view to argue with, for in spite of hypocritical denunciation of violence in the media, the raging street violence, crime, warfare, and violent action movies, TV shows and video games, indicates that the human race thrives on and revels in killing - no matter how much we may like to think otherwise."Straw Dogs" endorses Ardrey's world-view: the people of our out-of-the-way hamlet are easily driven to violence, their sins mostly overlooked by the hypocritically pious town leaders. Even a pacifist like David Sumner is not immune to the allure of murder; his claims of standing up principle (defending Niles, the murderous village idiot, from a mob) are dubious at best. The film has been read as a revenge fantasy a la Death Wish, which is ridiculous; Amy doesn't even tell David about the rape. Rather, it's a cumulative revenge; a man stuck in a small town with no friends, fed up with violence in his own culture, his taunting and torment here, his unhappy marriage to his wife, and his own weakness, allows his long-repressed rage to explode. In the end, even David Sumner is capable of horrific violence; not only that, he enjoys it. And the sheer visceral thrill of watching vile bad guys get handed their just deserts implicates us in the violence as well; none of us are innocent, and all of us are guilty.Peckinpah's direction is effective, presenting violence in all its glory and horror; he succeeds at showing an externally beautiful but inwardly hideous small town. The cast is good, if unspectacular: Dustin Hoffman embodies David Sumner as impotent professor and makes his transition frighteningly believable. Susan George is quite good as Amy, the confused, repressed young girl who married a guy who isn't right for her. The supporting cast is adequate, with T.P. McKenna and Del Henney giving the strongest performances as the well-meaning but ineffectual Sheriff and the most sympathetic of David and Amy's tormentors.Straw Dogs is a powerful, disturbing mediation on violence, with a power and force that few films a possess. It is a true masterpiece.10/10</fullReview>
	</review>

		<fullReview>Straw Dogs (1971) was a film that Sam Peckinpah directed during the early seventies. He adapted the screenplay from the thriller novel "The Siege at Trencher's Farm). 		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Sam Peckinpah's Brilliant Study of Psychosexual Turmoil</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"Heaven and earth are not humane, and regard the people as straw dogs." Lao-tzu,Chinese philosopher Dr. David Sumner (Dustin Hoffman) a brilliant young mathematician working on a scientific research grant, has traveled from America to the English Countryside near the village where his sensuous wife Amy (Susan George) grew up. David's stated purpose for moving is to find the peace, quiet and privacy to work on such complicated mathematics. However, David's real purpose is to escape the turmoil and violence of the late 60s United States, with it's demonstrations, riots and urban violence. However, David soon discovers he cannot escape turmoil and danger. It's everywhere: the pubs, the streets, the school yards, and, most importantly,inside himself.Within this framework, and the novel "The Siege at Trencher's Farm," screenwriter David Zelig Goodman and director Sam Peckinpah have fashioned a violent, suspenseful and often brilliant study of psycho-sexual turmoil. Each character represents some aspect of human nature. The Major (constable) represents authority and implied order. The Vicar represents organized religion, with all of its worldly shortcomimgs. Janice (Sally Thomsett), the young woman attracted to David represents youthful curiosity and feminine desire. Tom Hedden (Peter Vaugn), Janice's father, represents uncontrolled rage and mob mentality. Charlie (Del Henney) and Chris (Jim Norton) represent the light and dark sides of unsuppressed masculinity. The rat killer represents madness, and his mocking nature reminds us how close madness really. Henry Niles (David Warner), the mentally challenged man whose unintentional actions set the bloody siege in motion, represents innocence; which, with all its good intentions, can do great harm if unchecked by reason and caution. WARNING: SPOILERS David and Amy are also symbols, though they don't represent individual characteristics of psyche as they do two sides of a healthy, cohesive personality. David is dominated by reason, with it's prudent caution. Amy is dominated by emotion, with its spontaneity and willingness to take risks. Both David and Amy possess these characteristics and more. Since a healthy personality needs ALL these characteristics in proper balance, David's generally repressed emotions and Amy's repressed reason draw turmoil out of the environment. Their fear and uncertainty, which provoked David to leave the US, in particular, draws rage, humiliation and violence to them, for predators can smell fear. Most importantly, fearful people unconsciously act in manners which draw predators and violent situations to them.Only once David and Amy face ALL their emotions and self-doubts and prevail, do they progress hopefully toward the future. It's very significant that Amy saves David from the last attacker, demonstrating her emotions have finally been conquered by reason and courage. David expresses this most clearly in the last scene, as David drives Henry Niles home.Henry (CONFUSED): "I don't know my way home." David (SMILING, ENTHUSIASTIC): "Neither do I." END OF SPOILERS "Straw Dogs" is a violent, suspenseful and slightly gruesome motion picture. It's is also nothing short of brilliant in it's depiction of the cost of "controlling" one's emotions and hiding from responsibility for the sometimes, brutal consequences. It is nothing short of brilliant. I give "Straw Dogs" a "10".</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2004-05-14</date>
		<username>James Hitchcock</username>
		<score>0.5</score>
		<shortDescription>Muddled, incoherent and leaves a sour taste</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>`Straw Dogs' is one of a number of controversial British films of the early 1970s (others include `A Clockwork Orange' and `Get Carter') which started a national debate about censorship and the portrayal of violence in the cinema. At the centre of the film are David Sumner, a young American academic and his British wife Amy. David and Amy have left America to live in Amy's native village in the English West Country, hoping to find a quieter, more peaceful way of life. Their hopes, however, are to be cruelly disappointed. The local residents take a dislike to David and begin a campaign of harassment against the couple. Things come to a head one foggy night when the couple's car hits and injures Henry Niles, a mentally retarded villager. David and Amy take Niles back to their farmhouse in order to call a doctor to treat him, unaware that he has killed a local girl named Janice (either accidentally or otherwise). A lynch-mob led by Janice's father arrives at the house, demanding that the couple surrender Niles to them. David refuses, and when the mob tries to storm his house, he decides to fight back, meeting the mob's fury with a brutality of his own.It has been stated that `Straw Dogs' is not just a violent film but also a film about violence. Like a number of other films of the period, it depicts violent acts in a graphic way in order to pose fundamental questions about the social and psychological roots of violence and about its effects on society. There is, however, a difference between it and the two other films mentioned above. `Get Carter' and `Clockwork Orange' both deal with people for whom violence is a way of life, either professional gangsters or hardened hooligans. `Straw Dogs' deals with what happens when otherwise peaceful people turn violent; the inhabitants of a previously tranquil village turn into a raging mob, and a mild-mannered academic resists them with a cold, clinical ruthlessness.The film's main weakness is that it never makes these events seem really plausible. The locals seem to dislike David for a number of reasons, including a distrust of all outsiders, especially foreigners, and envy of his success and comparative wealth. Sexual jealousy also plays a part; although not particularly physically attractive, he has nevertheless won in marriage the most attractive girl from the village. One of the mob, Charlie Venner, is a former lover of Amy's, and several of the others would have liked to have been. These factors while they might give rise to simmering resentment, are not normally enough to provoke murderous fury, and the Niles plot (lifted from Steinbeck's `Of Mice and Men') was introduced to provide a motive for the mob to attack David's house.Unfortunately, however, this gives rise to further difficulties. It is, of course, a truism that the mildest of men is likely to resort to violence in order to protect his own life or that of a loved one, but David is never placed in that position. It is quite clear that he and Amy will be spared if Niles is handed over. It is, I feel, a very unusual man who, in order to protect a suspected murderer from vigilante justice, will turn on the vigilantes with sadistic fury, placing both himself and his wife in grave danger by so doing. There is nothing in the film which helps to explain why David, the puny, bespectacled academic should react in such a way. Moreover, it is clear that when the mob attack the house Janice's body has not been found, so they do not actually know that she is dead or that Niles has killed her; at that stage she is merely missing, so their desire for vengeance seems less credible. If the body had been found before these scenes take place, the mob's anger against Niles and anyone sheltering him would have been more understandable, but David's determination to protect the guilty man would have seemed all the more irrational.Another weakness in the film is its treatment of women. There are only two main female characters in the film, Amy and Janice, and both are shown provoking or colluding in their own sexual degradation or destruction. Janice is shown flirting provocatively with Niles, although he is known to be mentally unstable and suspected of being a dangerous pervert. In the infamous rape scene, Amy is forced into sex by Venner, but later appears to be deriving pleasure from the experience. I can well understand why feminists took such objection to this scene, and find those attempts that have been made to defend it unconvincing. Peckinpah's general outlook strikes me as being one of bleak misanthropy, but even in this context his view of female sexuality comes across as demeaning and misogynistic.I also disliked the view of country life given in the film. Certainly, country people in Britain, especially those from the West Country, are well used to being patronised by metropolitan sophisticates, but the savage caricature in this film goes well beyond the normal stereotypes of ignorant, slow-witted, straw-chewing yokels. Peckinpah seems to have imported into a British setting the American urban elite's view of the rural working class as violent, sexually perverted white trash.There are better things about the film. Although the first half is fairly slow moving, the scenes of the attack on the house are well handled, and Dustin Hoffman gives a convincing portrayal of both David the respectable academic and David as transformed into the violent fanatic. Susan George as Amy is able to suggest both a certain innocence and sexual desirability. There is material here that could have been part of a good action thriller. Peckinpah, however, clearly had an ambition to go beyond this and to make a statement about society. In my view he failed in this ambition. Whereas `Get Carter' offers us a clear-eyed condemnation of the criminal lifestyle, and `Clockwork Orange' is a masterly examination of the themes of good, evil and free will, `Straw Dogs' is a muddled, incoherent film that leaves a sour taste in the mouth. 5/10.</fullReview>
	</review>

You grow to hate the cornish folk who mock David and laugh behind his back. They even go and rape Amy while Hoffman's out snipe hunting. Someone has also murdered their cat, although by the end of the movie this is inconclusive to whom has done this. Finally Hoffman runs over Henry Niles, a village idiot played by an uncredited David Warner. After Hoffman takes Henry Niles home, the angry cornish folk come to take Henry and kill him. In the process while they break Hoffmans windows and kill the magistrate, Hoffman decides that he's going to fight back. In Sam Peckinpah style, limbs are blown off and the violence is absolutely brutal. By the end Hoffman has killed all of them. He is satisfied. He takes Henry Niles home. The movie is fantastic in exploring characters feelings and emotions. Charlie venner calls Amy 'Mrs Sumner' implying that he is mocking her, for she belongs to Hoffman now and not himself. The folk use their manners saying 'please' and 'thank you' to really hide the fact that they are much darker and inbred. Hoffman chickened out and trembling by the fact that his worst fear of crime has come back to kill him and make him suffer. Hoffman satisfied because he has beat this fear and ultimately killed it. The movie itself doesn't belong to a genre. But horror and thriller is the closest as it uses fog and darkness. Scenes are reminiscent of horror and thriller such as 'Night of the living dead' and 'Assault on Precinct 13'. Dustin Hoffman gives a strong and realistic performance as David Sumner and the rest of the cast do well to make him feel like trash. It's chilling and it's a really good movie. 9 out of 10.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>Straw Dogs is a complexly layered thematic film involving issues such as the true nature of violence, masculinity, territoriality and above all, the price we have to pay for displaying these naked emotions. But it's also an unbearably tense, gripping thriller. David Sumner retreats to rural Cornwall, to get away from street violence in America, only to find out that it exists in every place in the world, only in a different guise. The 'locals' (inhabited, imbred 'scum') pick on David for two simple reasons- he is a foreigner (well, American) and that they fancy his wife (understandable- it's Susan George).What makes the climax so inevitably true to life is how the locals attitude towards David starts off as mild hostility and gradually degenerates into full blown violence, who look for any excuse to terrorise David, his wife and his home. Much has been said about the rape scene, which, in this complete version is actually far less offensive. But what people don't often realise is that the two characters involved (Amy, David's wife and a scumbag local, Charlie) is that they already have a history together. Of course, rape is still rape and it is very shocking, even by todays standards, but considering that the two characters involved are far from being every day civilised human beings and are pretty screwed up- for them it may just be extremeley violent seduction, as when the second person rapes her-it appears that Amy does not enjoy this at all (this part was originally omitted from the cut version, which makes it's morals more ambiguous).Amy's feelings for Charlie (who for me isn't the definition of masculinity- more like an image of false macho, bouyish energy) have been so well hidden that the scene of violent seduction almost plays out as cartharsis, for both of them, which brings them rather oddly together (but as stated previousley, both are odd characters) and David is the only truly human person out of the lot (and more masculine than Charlie- he stands his ground when it needs to be stood- rather than resorting in false macho posturing).The fact that David is referred to as a coward by his wife says everything (does she have stronger feelings for Charlie because he projects himself to be more of a man? Typical!)- but he'll only fight back when its completely necassary, and when he does- well, see it for yourself. If any of this sounds like Fight Club, well, it must've been the equal, if not the surpassed version, released in 1971. Like most controversial films who are willing to make a bold statement, it all rings horribly true, which is probably what garners the respect of every artist, like Sam Peckinpah, to gain their status as a vital artist in their own medium.A MUST SEE for any film fan who likes their films to be bold rather than woossy, compromised tosh. Just don't show it to thick liberals, they'll never get it.</fullReview>

Peckinpah is a master film maker, and STRAW DOGS is one of his great works.</fullReview>

		<date>2004-02-25</date>
		<username>Senator_Corleone</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Most painful movie-watching experience of my life (spoilers)</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The title of Sam Peckinpah's 1971 film "Straw Dogs" is in reference to a type of ancient Chinese artifact that was worshiped, and then sacrificially burned. When seen in this context, the title makes perfect sense despite the fact that it is never mentioned by any of the characters. The movie is the story of how a normally meek, cowardly man "burns away" his personality and finds within himself the ability for violence and murder. When it was released, it was thrust to the forefront to the "violence in movies" debate alongside such films as "Dirty Harry" and "A Clockwork Orange." And it is, at times, quite violent. However, this film contains content that goes beyond mere violence to take it into another realm of shock. Watching "Straw Dogs" was the most painful viewing experience of my life. However, the fact that it is a necessary, important film is inescapable. Much has been made of how it is Peckinpah's statement on the nature of masculinity and manhood, complemented by his misogynistic, masochistic view of the world in general and women in particular. However, I believe there is another level it can be viewed on-that "Straw Dogs" is a nightmarish vision of the evil and beastial aspects inherent in every human being. It is a brutally harsh, cynical, and hateful cinema experience, routinely counted as one of the great disturbing movies along with films like "The Cook, the Thief, His Wife, and Her Lover" and "Battle Royale." Most films side with one set of characters against another. "Straw Dogs" does not. It despises all of the participants in its drama, from the "protagonist" David Summer (Dustin Hoffman in one of the great roles in his long, wonderful career) and his vixen wife Amy (Susan George in a brave, powerful performance) to the group of inbred English miscreants (a European counterpart to the Mountain Men in "Deliverance") who terrorize them. Peckinpah holds all of these people in contempt, and the particularly sadistic ending purges them all, in one way or another, from the system. There are two famous disturbing sequences in the movie. The first is the rape of Amy by two of the English hoodlums. This scene starts off innocently enough, with Charlie, Amy's former boyfriend, appearing at the Summer cottage. It soon turns ugly, however, as Charlie hits Amy and forces her into submission. During the actual act, Peckinpah uses quick cuts to tell the story in implied images. The action becomes something more than rape, however, when quick cuts to Amy's face seem to suggest she is enjoying what is happening to her. This was HUGELY controversial, angering women around the world. However, in the next few moments we see Amy ravaged again by Scut, Charlie's friend. Her horrified reaction to this development draws the line and shows Amy as a real woman, not a sex-addict (as many critics claimed the scene suggested). This is even more appalling when we realize that David never finds out about his wife's rape at the hands of the men. The second violent scene is the 30-minute finale. David, inadvertantly protecting a simpleton-giant named Niles (who is guilty of a completely different crime) in his house, fights off the gang as they try to break in to get at the large man. In this sequence, men are killed with shotguns, beaten to death with fire pokers, and (in a truly startling moment) garroted with a bear trap. While fighting, David gets no help from his parasitic, immature wife-who has turned to Charlie as her protector. Only when he slaps her does Amy listen to David. Is Peckinpah suggesting violence is the only way to get respect? That's what many contend. Also, the only sympathetic character in the entire film, the Colonel, is shot dead in cold blood by one of the drunken mob. After the massacre, David drives Niles back into town. "I don't know my way home," the large man tells the American. "Neither do I," is David's chilling answer, a smile curling over his lips. This movie stares angrily in the void of the human soul. It is unlike any of Peckinpah's other films, because, while many are very violent, he usually looked on his characters (who were killers, robbers, and bounty hunters) and found warmth and humanity in them. With "Straw Dogs," he looked upon his subjects and found only perversion, sickness, and hate.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2004-01-16</date>
		<username>ManhattanBeatnik</username>
		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Straw Dogs (1971)</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>In 1971, two of the most controversial films of all time were released within the same month. The first one, Stanley Kubrick's A Clockwork Orange, was very successful and is often cited as one of the best American pictures ever made. The other, Sam Peckinpah's Straw Dogs, has slowly slipped away into the unknown, and the few times it HAS been cited seemed to have always revolved around its controversy rather than its content. Which is a shame, because Straw Dogs is not only one of the finest American films ever made, but one of the finest films ever made period. It is Peckinpah's masterpiece. It is a brutal, powerful film that challenges the viewer to contemplate not only his beliefs but the value of his own life. It epitomized the renaissance of film that took place during the six-year period from 1967 to 1973, and it captures the physical and emotional intensity only before seen in foreign films. In it, Dustin Hoffman plays a mathematician who has moved with his wife (played stunningly by Susan George) to her small hometown in England. It is heavily hinted at that they were pacifists and left the United States due to riots involving the Vietnam war. Once in England, they hire a few locals to work on the garage of their cottage, and after a series of horrifying events, David (Hoffman) is forced to confront his own convictions in a shattering climax that leaves him (and the viewer) even more perplexed than in the beginning. The film is shot in cold, gray tones that emit a hopeless feeling. A sense of dread, isolation, and desperation is conveyed in every frame. There is not a single comfortable or joyous scene to be found in the entire picture; even laughter seems ominous when filtered through Peckinpah's dark vision. All of this will most likely turn the viewer off to the movie ... and it should. Peckinpah doesn't want you to feel comfortable while watching Straw Dogs. He wants you to fidget in your seat, to want to look away but not be able to. Some might find this manipulative or even sadistic, but in truth it is nothing of the sort. In truth, the story of the film is so cold and dark that it demands a like tone. Why make such a film, you might ask? If there is no hope whatsoever, what is the point of subjecting the viewer to two hours of misery? Because at the heart of the film lies a portrayal of humanity, despair, and soul-searching that has never been equaled before or since. While watching the film, one would be quick to label the intruders of the couple's cottage the enemies, but the horrific truth is that David himself is equally dangerous. Throughout the picture, David displays an almost unbearable amount of hidden hostility and emotional tension. His "playful" spoken attacks on his wife are just as harmful as the intruders' physical attacks against her, which is where many critics began to misinterpret the meaning of the film. A number of reviews described Straw Dogs as the descent of a withdrawn man into the beast-like instincts within him, which is utterly ridiculous. Because at heart, David is not a pacifist; while he is certainly revolted by physical violence, he displays a tremendous amount of hostility and even sadism in more subtle forms. The ending, in which he finally resorts to physical attack, is only an external demonstration of violent feelings that have been there all along. And it is there that Peckinpah takes his biggest stance against violence itself. In his most successful film, The Wild Bunch, Peckinpah's trademarked violence was unintentionally glorified by the western genre; graphic as it was, the violence in that film was deemed acceptable because the genre itself was larger-than-life. In Straw Dogs, the violence cannot be interpreted as anything but horrific. And there is, indeed, much violence in the film ... but not in the form one might expect. In Straw Dogs, we see violence of EVERY form: implied hostility, sexual intrusion, and extreme physical attack. One could argue the significance of all this for hours on end and never reach a conclusion, and I don't think Peckinpah would want anyone to try and give one single meaning to Straw Dogs. But his use of avant-garde techniques (short slow-motion cuts), creative sound editing (keep your ear in tune to the children's party favors at the church social), and tension (so much is built throughout the picture that when it's finally released in the climax, you can barely breathe) make Straw Dogs an American cinematic landmark. When David is driving away into the night at the end of the picture and is told by his passenger, "I don't know my way home," he replies, "That's okay. I don't either." Walking away from the film, you will feel equally disoriented, but you will have been somewhere and will never forget it.Grade: A+</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Ultimately ludicrous.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"Straw Dogs" begins with one of the slowest build-ups (almost an hour long) you're ever likely to come across in a movie. Fortunately, Dustin Hoffman's superb acting holds our interest throughout this section. Then we get to the "notorious" rape scene, which is not half as offensive as it's reputed to be. Peckinpah has often been accused of being a misogynist, yet he surprised me here by portraying the shattering emotional after-effects of the rape; no way you could conclude that ultimately Susan George had "enjoyed" the experience. The real - and ultimately fatal - problem with the film is the totally misconceived climax. (SPOILERS FOLLOW) Basically, for the last 40 minutes or so, we are watching Hoffman trying to protect his house from an angry lynch mob, after refusing to surrender to them a man that he had previously hit with his car, and who may have had something to do with the disappearance of a young girl. What we know all along - but Hoffman doesn't - is that this wounded man is actually a killer. And that does put us in the uncomfortable position of asking: "What's the point? Why should so much blood be shed over the fate of an individual we don't care about at all?" (END OF SPOILERS). The violence, although not graphic at all by today's standards, reaches such "operatic" heights that it becomes ludicrous. Pechinpah even stoops to the gimmick of bringing a seemingly dead man back to life for yet another bloody struggle. And throughout all this, his herky-jerky editing style produces nausea. Of course, that may have been the intended effect after all. (**)</fullReview>

		<username>Jacob Rosen (bix171@comcast.net)</username>
		<score>0.5</score>
		<shortDescription>Hmmm...</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Left unchecked, Sam Peckinpah's penchant for explicit violence betrays him as a sadistic voyeur hiding behind intellectual arguments that dictate cinematic violence as a necessary evil. That may be--his `The Wild Bunch' and `Bring Me The Head Of Alfredo Garcia' are good examples where it is--but here the brutality is predicated on the specious beliefs that reason is useless in the face of violence and responsive violence is better than the original threat. Dustin Hoffman plays a mild-mannered mathematician who moves to his wife's rural English village in search of peace and quiet and finds menace in the form of the townsfolk. Hoffman seems to be still in his Benjamin Braddock mode, observing everything with a bemused, babyish look on his face and he simply wears you down with his stubbornness to change even when danger is obvious; when he finally does change, his motivations seem extreme to say the least. (A man's home is his castle and needs to be defended at all costs.) Perhaps Peckinpah's most disturbing observations, however, are about women: not only are they seen as temptresses and dimwits, but their malicious acts of betrayal (particularly from Hoffman's wife, played by Susan George) indicate they pose more of a threat to Peckinpah's masculinity than any male interloper. Though his technique has always seemed on the sloppy side (usually forgivable given his focus on concepts rather than artfulness), here, outside of a couple of TV-style flourishes, Peckinpah is in control, filming many of his interior backgrounds on a tilt to give the characters' point of view a swooning sense of disorientation. The music is by Jerry Fielding and while it's mostly bad, it does come through when it has to.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>(gsygsy)</username>
		<score>0.5</score>
		<shortDescription>Watch another movie</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>It's interesting to compare STRAW DOGS to Cronenberg's A HISTORY OF VIOLENCE, with which has a lot in common thematically. Peckinpah presents the viciousness of the male world as unavoidable - Hoffman's character can't escape it, even in the apparently idyllic surroundings of Cornwall. Cronenberg's protagonist is also trying to hide from brutality but, as in STRAW DOGS, it comes calling, and a choice has to be made. There are of course many other differences of plot and emphasis between the two films but it seems to me that the key difference is that Cronenberg is so much more sympathetic to his central female character. She is a person in her own right, not just an adjunct, not just a provider of sexual services. Mrs Sumner in STRAW DOGS is like the girl in OF MICE AND MEN (to which Peckinpah self-consciously refers).STRAW DOGS is, yes, very powerful, in a hitting-you-over-the-head kind of way, and, yes, it is an exploration of the values of conventional masculinity, but in the end Hoffman goes off into the sunset, having found his essential warrior-self, in the company of David Warner's character who pretty much represents uncensored desire. Viggo Mortensen in the Cronenberg film purges himself with water after his gore-fest, and returns home, where things can never be the same again. In short, Peckinpah's is a two-dimensional glamorisation of traits in half the world's population, and he doesn't seem to give a damn about any active contribution that the other half makes - like Grace Kelly in HIGH NOON (another self-conscious quote), all Susan George can do is submit to the male mafioso morality. Cronenberg's is a much more powerful and carefully thought out examination of how these traits affect men AND women. Peckinpah's film is full of powerful shots and great editing - the man is a fine film-maker. But as a philosopher of violence, on the basis of this movie, he seems to me to be so in love with his subject matter that he is in no position to mount a critique of it. It leaves a nasty taste in the mouth. Watch the Cronenberg. Or, for something more glib, but nevertheless entertaining, David Fincher's FIGHT CLUB.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Compelling and disturbing</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"You really don't care, do you?" This fascinating, brilliant film from Sam Peckinpah is compelling and nightmarish, a blend of rural horror and the western, only set in the English countryside. It's not just well-crafted, stomach-churning entertainment, it's about human nature and the realisation of human nature. David Sumner (Dustin Hoffman) is a passive, friendly American mathematician, who has moved with his wife Amy (Susan George), to her old house on the outskirts of a small English coastal town, so that he can have peace to think and work on his book. Almost instantly however, he's met with hostility by the rowdy locals, who start to play games with him and taunt him, actively abusing his wife to cause conflict between them, as "pacifist" David is pressured to act by Amy, who knows full well what is going on and is angered by David's so-what denial. Eventually things come to a head in the film's most notorious scene, and they reach breaking point. Finally an unfortunate turn of events forces David to act. There is no real ambiguity about David Sumner for us, only for him. It's clear from the start, from Hoffman's performance, in a bar scene in which boorish, loud drunkard Tom Hedden (Peter Vaughan) gets violent when he's refused a drink, that David is thrilled by the spectacle, although he does not acknowledge this himself, preferring to live a cosy, comfortable, peaceful ideal that is false. It's fitting that he's a mathematician, a rationalist trying to make sense of things with numbers and figures on a blackboard. Indeed David Sumner's non-confrontational attitude seems like just another cold form of dominance and aggression, and effectively provokes the men working on his garage. The educated man may just be the best barbarian of them all. In the final siege of the house, David outsmarts the invaders in clever and sadistic ways, blowing Tom Hedden's foot off, and throwing a man-trap onto Charlie Venner's (Del Henney) head. All of this seems cheerfully automatic, like a dance, and David even plays music at one point. There's no real character arc for David as he hasn't really changed, only his sense of self. In the final scenes he sort of moves around loosely, smiling, having received catharsis from the violence, release from his frustration, and maybe some truth. It's an excellent performance from Dustin Hoffman. Susan George is also excellent as Amy, and has a great dynamic with Hoffman, no doubt helped by the fact that they spent some time with each other before filming. She's convincingly wild and savage, particularly in the final scenes, shattering David's ideal of the woman he married, and throughout the film she exercises her own kind of dominance over David, finally pushing him to fight back. As they settle in, any notion of settling is dissolved to reveal an all-pervasive violence, and nature wins as they eventually both succumb to their primal instincts and to necessity. The film is directed with great flair and style by Sam Peckinpah. The countryside is as beautiful as the desert landscapes of The Wild Bunch, only cold and harsh, and it has much in common with the western in the way it uses the landscape. Even the story could have been a western. The film is very atmospheric; extra mist was poured onto the moors for the finale. The film is shot in a very cold, stark way, distanced somewhat from the performers. Some scenes are shot and edited in such a way that makes them disturbingly effective, such as the party scene in the church, in which Amy has flashbacks to her violation inter-cut with children and adults blowing party horns. Almost all of the extras seem like frenzied animals. The supporting cast is great and for the most part very menacing, but natural. Peter Vaughan, Ken Hutchison, Del Henney and Jim Norton are all excellent, and there's a small role from David Warner as Henry Niles, a man shunned by the town folk for having problems. The film's title comes from Chinese philosopher Lau-Tzu - "Heaven and Earth are not human, and regard the people as straw dogs." The opening scene, descending from a grey sky on to a scene of children running around and playing, suggests that we are just animals who don't know what we are or why, and that violent sacrifice is natural and necessary.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Slow-moving and overrated... but builds up to a superb, violent climax</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Sam Peckinpah was a notoriously angry and aggressive man who often conflicted with studios, actors and fellow film-crew. So it's no surprising that he is renowned for his violent films, particularly his 1969 Western "The Wild Bunch", which irrevocably changed the Western - and cinema - forever for it's depiction of on-screen bloody violence. So, we have Peckinpah to thank for the violent cinema we have today (I'm not being sarcastic). Personally, I don't think much of his films. "The Wild Bunch" didn't do much for me, although I did admire it's innovative and daring violence which was something for it's time. I was disappointed with "Cross of Iron", his WW2 picture about the German's on the Eastern Front. Peckinpah himself had served with the US Marine Corps during WW2, however, to his disappointment, he never actually saw combat. He gained a lot of bad attention for himself, and his 1971 film "Straw Dogs" brought the bad publicity to a whole new level, as it contains a very controversial scene where a woman seems to enjoy being raped. I won't go to much into this, as it's incredible how much this has been talked about. However, I'll just say that personally I found it to be a disgusting, poorly made scene, for the simple fact that it was just plain ludicrous, as well as offensive towards women. Rape is not something that should be portrayed as it is here. Look towards films like "I Spit on Your Grave" and "The Last House on the Left" for it's true realistic and harrowing nature. Interestly, "Straw Dogs" was released the same year as fellow controversial films "Dirty Harry" and "A Clockwork Orange". Sweet Jesus, that was some year for the censorship board! Dustin Hoffman (In a very surprising role - he later admitted that he only took the part because he needed the money) is a mathematician who moves to the home village of his English wife Amy. The couple have run away from American in the height of increasing social unrest - but if they thought it was bad in the States... oh boy! It isn't long at all before the small village, depicted as being inhabited by about twenty people, most of whom are your stereotypical inbred scum who drink and perv all day, become aware of the Yank and the local girl moving in. The local hoodlums are led by Susan's old boyfriend (Del Henney), who resent their arrival right away, despite all them drooling over the attractive Amy. The hoodlums offer to do repairs on the couple's house, but it isn't long before they begin harassing them, and because David is so timid and reluctant when it comes to confrontation, it isn't long before their cat is killed and hung in their wardrobe, Susan is raped, and when a child molester/killer is on the run from the gang, David takes him in to defend him... and finally, he decides to take a stand and protect his home and his wife. "I will not allow violence against this house..." Carnage ensues.Peckinpah certainly takes his time here... which is the problem I had with his later film "Cross of Iron". It's tedious at times, as the first hour is dedicated to a slow building of tension in order to release an explosive conclusion and confrontation at the end. It's worth sitting through just to see that, as the push-over David finally grows a pair and fights back; fortifying his house and picking the drunken ruffians off one by one after it becomes apparent that he will be killed, along with Susan. Hoffman is extremely out of place in this film, however, he gives a superb performance and I really could not have pictured someone else in the role. Susan George, I found, was extremely irritating and overall, Amy and David seemed like the most unlikeliest couple ever! The screenplay is very questionable at times, especially when it comes to the 'rape' scene which descends into a complete farce when Amy appears to 'enjoy' it... at the beginning she tries to feebly fight him off, before kissing and hugging her old boyfriend who is performing the assault on her. However, the second assault which follows straight after is without a doubt rape. This is all poor directing and work on Peckinpah's part... like what the hell was he thinking? This is just disgusting, and surely, as an educated man, he could have foreseen the consequences of such a scene? Yes... the film was banned for 18 years in the UK! "Straw Dogs" is a decent enough film, although I don't think I'd be too pushed on watching it again. It's themes of resentment towards outsiders and isolation are engulfed by the overwhelming odour of what it really is: a macho-fantasy in which masculinity is measured by how you abuse women and by how much you can drink down at the local. Whereas David, the intelligent but timid and cowardly man, only proves his masculinity when he single-handedly takes the law into his own hands and brutally kills the men who are trying to take a child killer into custody! It's pure Pekinpah alright; take a story and push as many buttons and pull as many strings and see how much you can anger certain fractions of society. Did I enjoy it? Well, I was bored for a good bit of it, but overall, I liked it because of Hoffman's performance and for the brilliant, violent confrontation at the end. A film worthy of it's controversy... but far from brilliant.</fullReview>

		<date>2011-09-15</date>
		<username>zardoz-13</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>"Straw Dogs" Has Teeth!!!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Controversial filmmaker Sam Peckinpah forsook his characteristic Old West setting in "Straw Dogs" to depict how the most harmless milquetoast in the universe can metamorphose into a man of violence. Basically, what you have in Peckinpah's sixth film is the theme of survival of the fittest. British author Gordon Williams' novel "The Siege at the Trencher's Farm" served as the film's literary source. According to Peckinpah biographer David Wheddle," Peckinpah changed the title of the film to "Straw Dogs" based on Chinese philosopher Lao Tzu's quote in "The Book of 5,0000 Characters." The passage Peckinpah cited is "Heaven and Earth are ruthless, and treat the myriad creatures as straw dogs: the sage is ruthless and treated the people as straw dogs. ... Is not the space between Heaven and Earth like a bellows?" Weddle doesn't identify the individual who gave Peckinpah the inspiration for the quotation. According to Marshal Fine and Garner Simmons in their respective Peckinpah biographies "Blood Sam" and "Peckinpah: A Portrait in Montage," actor Walter Kelly furnished the director with Tzu's quote. Initially, writes Peckinpah biographer Garner Simmons, "Logan's Run" scenarist David Z. Goodman penned two early drafts of the script. Goodman told Simmons about the differences between the book and the film. One of the minor changes was that the novel's protagonist George Magruder was an English professor rather than an astrophysicist. The biggest change, however, was no rape occurred in the novel. Furthermore, according to Simmons, Williams hated Peckinpah's use of sex, not violence.Mild-mannered American mathematician David Sumner (a bespectacled Dustin Hoffman of "The Graduate") takes a sabbatical in tranquil Cornwall, England, far away from the violence and unrest in America so he can work on a grant. When the villagers ask him if he saw any of this turmoil, David says that he only saw the violence between the commercials on television. The rural Cornwall village, where "Straw Dogs" takes place, is the hometown of his slutty wife Amy (Susan George of "Lola"), and their marriage appears to be deteriorating because his Lolita-type spouse wants him to pay more attention to her than work on his equations. Simmons quotes Hoffman who wasn't entirely sold on either Susan George's casting as well as the notion that David would have married such a shallow-minded woman. Indeed, Amy traipses around the village with no bra on as he brings her husband the gift of a man-trap used in the old days to catch poachers. Amy excites the locals with her no-bra-look. As it turns out, one of the locals is none other than an old boyfriend Charlie Venner (Del Henney of "Brannigan") who Amy hasn't seen in six years. Charlie helps David load the man-trap into his convertible Triumph coupe. David decides to hire Charlie to help Norman Scutt (Ken Hutchison of "Ladyhawke") complete work on the roof of his garage. These ruffians are more intent on watching Amy parade around bare-chested than perform their chores. Meantime, the locals are not sure about how to deal with David. Of course, the local constable, Major John Scott (T.P. McKenna of "Red Scorpion"), and the Reverend Barney Hood (Colin Welland of "Villain"), treat David with kindness, but Tom Hedden (Peter Vaughan of "The Mackintosh Man") and his relatives have nothing but contempt for him. Eventually, Tom's evil relatives and their friends take David on a duck hunting trip and abandon him in the wilderness, while Charlie and his mate Norman rape Amy. First, Charlie rapes Amy in a lovingly tender fashion, but Norman shares none of Charlie's sentiments. Meantime, a simpleton, Harry Niles (David Warner of "The Omen") arouses trouble because he was not jailed for a sex crime. Everything comes to a head after a church party when Tom's slutty daughter Janice Hedden (Sally Thomsett of "Baxter!") tries to take advantage of Harry because David wants nothing to do with her. Tom has warned Henry's brother about him.The simple-minded Henry and Janice retire to a barn for their assignation. Poor Janice has no idea that she is playing with fire. Henry kills her in a scene straight out of "Of Mice and Men" with a conspicuous lack of emotion. Later, David hits Henry with his car on the way home from the church party. A drunken Tom and his mates show up at David's house and demand that he hand over Henry. David refuses and chaos ensues. When Major Scott tries to intervene, Tom kills him by accident and things get out of hand. Amy wants David to hand over Henry to Tom and company, but our hero refuses. At the same time, he tries to maintain his nonviolence, but Tom and company sorely try him until he retaliates and everybody but David, Amy, and Henry survive. Dustin Hoffman delivers a dynamic performance and Peter Vaughn makes a nefarious villain.Several scenes stand out by themselves. For example, Peter Vaughan's first scene in the bar when he calls for another pint and raises hell until he gets it is vigorous stuff. The scene when Hoffman careens in his Triumph between a truck and a bull dozer approaching each other when him slipping between them is exciting. Peckinpah orchestrated the final quarter-hour for maximum suspense and tension. The bloodiest that "Straw Dogs" gets is when a lout blows his foot off with his own double-barreled shotgun. Peckinpah builds up the narrative so that we are aware of what the opposition looks like and makes Hoffman look so naive and harmless that you relish the outcome when he defeats his enemies. Peckinpah foreshadows the use of a giant steel mantrap and his use of violence is still grisly but it seems toned-down but realistic compared with his classic western "The Wild Bunch." The ambiguous ending will prompt many interpretation. As David is driving the retarded Niles home, Niles says, "I don't know my way home." David replies, "That's okay, neither do I."</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>The Most Powerfully Intelligent Thriller of the 70's ...</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"Straw Dogs" ignites a flame that centuries of civilization didn't extinguish, reminding us that it takes more than a biological asset to make a man. Manhood is less a pride to be preached than a virtue to be reached and "Straws Dogs" is Sam Peckinpah's masterwork honoring the idea of a necessary violence revealing both cowardice and bravery. This Darwinian issue is tackled in such an exhilarating way we wonder if he's condemning or celebrating it."Straw Dogs" is a harrowing contemplation of a man's gradual metamorphosis. A man who wants peace so much, until he realizes that peace is a privilege that cannot be obtained without fighting. Dangerous or deadly, at least fighting guarantees self-respect, and one cannot inspire respect without self-respect. We're not men, we become that. And we can't live in the illusory comfort of feeling respected just because we're called "sir". Sooner or later, we face our own condition as men. And that's why, more than a psychological thriller, "Straw Dogs" is an existential masterpiece.And the character who crystallizes both our frustrations and our empathy is Dustin Hoffman as David Summer, an American mathematician, married to a young, blonde English girl, Amy, played by the beautiful Susan George. She's the slut of the small rural town they live in, a quite hostile environment for David, whose intelligence and awkward mannerisms, makes him a believable stereotypical nerd, incapable of any sociability. A strange man and a stranger, the perfect target for a progressively growing harassment, provoking so many embarrassing interactions and transforming David's mind into a psychic ticking bomb.And compared to these English hunks, David exudes cowardice and a total lack of virility inherited from a whole life of studies and quietness ? as if David spent most of his time, mourning the loss of manhood caused by a meaningless life. Like the archetypal Peckinpah's character, he's a loner waiting for redemption. His patience emphasizes a passive attitude, as if he was waiting for things to loosen up. But the provocation only goes worse, like when his harassers hang the cat in his bedroom's closet, proving that he doesn't even control his intimacy? and this is only a warning before the ultimate humiliation.And fate couldn't have picked a more convenient tool, with Amy, the trophy wife envied by the village men. The more they got used to David's unmanly behavior, the more it reinforced the idea that he didn't deserve his wife. And this is where Sam Peckinpah started making enemies through this film, by suggesting that we still live in a prehistoric era were it takes muscles to have the girl, not brains. But this is a misinterpretation because it's not a matter of muscles either, but of guts. And yes, women despise gut-less men. This is the controversial aspect of the film illustrated by the ambiguous yet, memorable rape scene.Yes, it's disturbing because it's one of the bravest moments ever captured on film. It doesn't deny the truth that rape is one sick and abject act, but, it also helps to the characterization of Amy, a girl who's so shocked by her husband's lack of virility that she found like a sort of relief from living a brief but intense moment where she feels the strength of a man, the strength that could only go by the act of overpowering her. The rape was the climactic aspect of her constantly teasing exhibitions to make David jealous, and the involuntary consequence of a rather childish attitude.But I'm glad Sam left out the ambiguity when came the second rape, more disturbing and brutal. Because there was no implicitness anymore, Amy was a victim and we could blame David for having been fooled again by going hunting. At that point, a castrated David realized that handling a gun and shooting a bird didn't make him more a man and that he was the buffoon of a stupid farce with a poor bird as a collateral damage and Amy, the real prey. David did it wrong, but for the last time. At that point, men were either savage beasts or weak and gut-less, and women objects of 'possession'. Talk about an optimistic view on humanity. But this is the genius of "Straw Dogs"' characterization: only an extreme situation could highlight the ruthless ferocity of human condition and make the climax even more rewarding, when David finally reaches his breaking point, incarnated by the iconic sight of his glasses' broken lens.And the apotheosis of all this repressed anger is a bloody massacre where David defends his honor, his wife, and his territory as if his life depended on it, until his life really depended on it. Never believing in God, David believed in instinct, but ironically overcame his enemies in a biblical poetry, as if he was David with his brains as a sling versus an outnumbering Goliath. But Goliath and God were dead in that celebration of violence whose graphicness was only equaled by the disturbing rape scene. We're far from the poetry of "The Wild Bunch" climactic shootout: here, the deaths are painful and ugly like symbolizing the struggle to conquer one's own pride. Not about 'dying in a blaze of glory' but resurrecting from the dirt. No conquest without danger, no redemption without conquest, no life without passion. Peckinpah paved a bloody way for the future Scorsese's and Tarantino's.Unfairly labeled as fascist by some pompous critics, "Straw Dogs" is on the contrary, one of the most powerfully intelligent thrillers ever made, working on a Darwinist, existential and finally extraordinary Nietschian level where one man must obey his courage to fight or die? and it's so fascinating that it also works on a self-reflexive away, since this is a movie about the guts it takes to be a man ? to make his point about his own idea of manhood, that's exactly what Sam Peckinpah, a bloody genius, did.</fullReview>

		<username>Sheldonshells</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>A Good statement on a grim reality</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Upon first viewing, Straw Dogs seemed a little bit over-hyped to me. I read quite a few reviews and commentaries on it (from the past and present) and so decided to check it out for myself. After I watched it a few more times, concentrating on it more closely and getting more absorbed into the themes, the storyline, the character nuances and pathos, the male/female interactions, etc, and finally deciding that it was indeed a quality film. There has been much said about its depiction of the supposed nature of masculinity and femininity (the latter not well showcased, by Susan George's character or by the sexually adventurous young female local who is accidentally killed by a mentally handicapped man), but it's really the masculine aspect that is focused on in any real depth. The contrast between Hoffman's new age, more gentle, maleness and the rustic and more traditional, brutish, "conscienceless" masculinity displayed by the local men is an often entertaining juxtaposition (and let's not forget the more balanced, level-headed, reasonable yet still tough and authoritative manhood of the local sheriff in the equation). The film is obviously making a statement about the violent nature of humanity and men in particular, showing the viewer different types of masculinity and showing how, supposedly, Hoffman's more new age type doesn't hold up to nature, because ultimately Mr. Sumner (Hoffman's character) proves that he is not so evolved after being constantly harassed and provoked by the more brutish men. This can be taken in many ways: nature overcoming nurture, a man pushed to the brink, a man merely trying to protect his home and wife from invaders,etc; but the film overlooks the very powerful forces of socialization that have a part to play in shaping people, and instead believes that "nature" has all the say, when that is just not true.The violence still stands up to today's films, especially the sexual violence, which actually goes much further than many PC/feminist whipped, cowardly mainstream (or even indie) filmmakers of today would allow or be allowed(almost as if they want to make us believe that rape doesn't exist). Therefore, the film hasn't lost its relevance in that respect, as many might believe. As for it's effect on me, I actually find it indescribably sad that such brutish louts are still around in great number today, but of course this kind of toxic socialization of men hasn't yet stopped, and has almost, very unfortunately, increased slightly since the Reagon/Mulrooney era. I say this because this film, consciously or not, gives us a look at what this kind of socialization of men can lead to: a pack of , disrespectful, thoughtless, malignant 'straw dogs' that leads to irrational and brutal killing sprees that offer no shining light into a more sophisticated way of dealing with situations--but of course that is just a part of reality, and the film dealt with this troubling and dangerous reality quite well. I've read many reviews on this film and, surprisingly, none have commented on this angle. So perhaps even though Hoffman's character may be described as "wussy" or "castrated" (which, of course, he ultimately proves he is not) maybe if the other men were a little more like him (or least like the sheriff) then the world would be a much better, safer, place in which to live.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<fullReview>There's a moment in Sam Peckinpah's Straw Dogs where petulant brat Susan George gets out of her car in front of the old chum townies she and milquetoast husband Dustin Hoffman have hired to repair their cottage in rural England, and her skirt hikes up, exposing her underwear. She goes out of her way to expose herself (and again, when she strips off her top and stands in front of a window, loose breasts swinging), then glares angrily at the townies for their staring.George's Amy is the perfect woman for women-haters everywhere. She's irresponsible and foolish, victim and participant, immature and demanding of respect. This character drives Peckinpah's hateful psychological mishmash along to its inevitable and predictable conclusion, a ghastly climax wherein pacifist Hoffman has to defend his retarded neighbor and feckless wife from a full-frontal assault on home and hearth.Hoffman is such a miserable coward that we sit astonished at what new humiliation he will rationalize, while he sucks down cigarettes as if there was something more than tobacco in them. The townies are giggling, smelly, whiskey-swilling louts who seem genuinely peeved that George doesn't want to strike up her old relationships with them (Hoffman is such a weenie that the viewer starts to ask the same question).But, considering Susan George's sour countenance and lush, natural attributes, you know that she's going to pay for her fecklessness. She gets pseudo-raped, wrapping herself around her first attacker to pull as much manliness and aggression from him as she can (remember, she's married to Hoffman). Then the second townie shows up for his turn.Herein lies Peckinpah's underlying hatred of his female character. She is everything men love physically and hate intellectually and emotionally. He's saying that woman are bigger louts than men, therefore, to quote a line from Blazing Saddles, it's okay to work up a "Number Six" on them.Straw Dogs is quite possibly the ugliest Peckinpah ever made, even more than Pat Garrett and Billy the Kid. There, you just get to see chickens get their heads blown off.Lucky chickens--if Peckinpah's vision of humanity is the realistic one.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Stray Puppy</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Add Peckinpah to the front of your list of people who pushed the "aesthetic extreme" in American cinema. Following him comes Bakshi and Ollie Stone...and I think you know the rest. Unlike his followers, Peckinpah could never get good production values for his work. Hence they all look cheap and used (and not in the good way). This movie, as with 'Wild Bunch', is of historical interest. I personally find Peckinpah's method too simple. He is all about zooms and too often his camera is tethered to characters. Seriously, you can call this an early Scorsese movie. For my imagination, I just wonder how Malick or Jewison would've handled it.Two things make this interesting: the first is Jerry Fielding's unnatural score. It isn't bad, but it creates the illusion of an alien world in ways that Peckinpah himself cannot.The second is Peckinpah's contribution to the Eisenstein montage. Most of the "extremists" have adopted it ever since. I guess that involves less thinking for them.Once again, Sam hits on religion. This time using science (and some concepts of evolution). If you do watch this, follow it up with 'Midnight Express'...same themes, different people.</fullReview>
